Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Agent's Return of Defective Slave: Two Narrations from al-Mabsut
Intra-Hanafi dispute with talīl and refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two conflicting Hanafi narrations regarding the effect of an agent's acknowledgment of a defect without litigation and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
An agent's acknowledgment does not make the principal liable; the slave returns only to the agent per the general narration.

This passage from al-Mabsut presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. When an agent sells a slave that later proves defective, and the agent acknowledges the defect without going to court, does the slave automatically revert to the principal? The answer depends on which narration one follows. The first narration treats the return as 'determined' (muta'ayyin) – as if the judge had ruled – thus binding the principal. The general narration, however, treats the transaction as a new sale between agent and buyer, so the slave only returns to the agent. Sahib al-Hidayah critically refutes the first view, helping us understand the school's preferred position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Riwāyāt
Locate the two conflicting positions: one narration from Kitāb al-Buyūʿ of al-Mabsūt claims the return is determined (mutaʿayyin) and binds the principal without litigation. The general narrations say the slave returns only to the agent.
The first is a solitary narration; the second is from the majority riwāyāt of al-Mabsūt.
2
Examine the First Narration's Argument
Understand why it holds the return is binding: the defect could not have arisen in the buyer's possession, so the agent and buyer's act mimics the judge's ruling. Therefore, the principal is bound as if a court had ordered the return.
The key term here is mutaʿayyin – 'determined' or 'inevitable'.
3
Examine the General Narration's Argument
The acknowledgment creates an exchange sale (masakh bayʿ) between agent and buyer, which for third parties (including the principal) is a new sale (bayʿ jadīd). Thus, the principal is not a party and cannot be bound.
This distinction between parties and outsiders is a Hanafi hallmark.
4
Analyze al-Hidāyah's Refutation
Sahib al-Hidāyah rejects the 'determined return' argument. He explains that the buyer's right is initially to a sound item; when a defect appears, the right shifts to returning the item only if no new defect has arisen. Thus, return is not automatic – it is conditional. Hence the proof fails.
This refutation strengthens the general narration's position.
5
Identify the Muftā Bihi
Based on al-Hidāyah's criticism and the fact that the general narration is from the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, the relied-upon position is that the slave returns only to the agent, who must compensate the buyer and cannot proceed against the principal.
The first narration is considered weak (marjūḥ) within the school.

اس کی دلیل سابق میں گذر چکی ہے کہ اقرار کی وجہ سے بغیر قضاء کے غلام کی واپسی وکیل اور مشتری کے حق میں اگر چہ مسخ بیج ہے لیکن ان کے علاوہ کے حق میں بیج جدید ہے۔ اور موکل ان دونوں کا غیر ہے۔ پس ان دونوں کے درمیان منعقدہ بیج ان کے غیر یعنی موکل پر کیوں لازم ہوگی یعنی موکل پر یہ غلام لازم نہ ہوگا۔
صاحب ہدایہ نے لان الرد متعین کا جواب دیتے ہوئے فرمایا کہ آپ کا یہ کہنا کہ غلام کا ر داور واپس کیا جانا متعین ہے۔ غلط ہے۔ اسلئے کہ مشتری کا حق اولا وصف سلام میں ثابت ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی مشتری صحیح سالم بیع کا مستحق ہوتا ہے۔ لیکن جب مبیع صحیح سالم نہ ہو بلکہ امیں عیب ہو تو اسکا حق مبیع کو واپس کرنے کی طرف منتقل ہو جاتا ہے۔ یعنی مشتری کیلئے مبیع واپس کرنے کا حق ثابت ہو جاتا ہے۔لیکن اگر سابق عیب کے علاوہ مبیع کے اندر مشتری کے پاس نیا عیب بھی پیدا ہو گیا یا سابقہ عیب میں اضافہ ہو گیا تو اس صورت میں مشتری کیلئے مبیع واپس کرنے کا حق نہیں ہوتا۔ بلکہ مشتری اپنے نقصان کے بقدر ثمن واپس لے سکتا ہے۔ یعنی جو عیب بائع کے پاس پیدا ہوا تھا۔ اس کی وجہ سے غلام کی قیمت میں جو کمی واقع ہو گی۔ مشتری اس مقدار کو بائع سے لینے کا مجاز ہو گا۔ بہر حال یہ بات ثابت ہو گئی کہ عیب کی وجہ سے مبیع واپس کرنا متعین نہیں ہے اور جب واپس کرنا متعین نہیں ہے تو آپ کی بیان کردہ دلیل لان الرد متعین بھی درست نہ ہوگی ۔
صاحب ہدایہ کہتے ہیں کہ اس مسئلہ کو کفایہ میں اس سے زیادہ تفصیل کے ساتھ بیان کیا ہے۔
Chapter of Agency

According to one narration from al-Mabsut's Book of Sales, this return becomes binding upon the principal without litigation. That is, when the buyer returns the defective slave to the agent, it is returned to the principal. And for this, the agent does not need to engage in litigation. Because when the defect is such that its like could not have arisen while in the buyer's possession, then returning the slave due to this defect is determined (muta'ayyin). That is, both the agent and the buyer have done exactly what the judge does: if the agent and the buyer had litigated before the judge regarding such a defect whose like could not have arisen, the judge would return the slave to the agent, and by that ruling it would be returned to the principal, and the agent would not have to bear the trouble of litigation. In any case, when the agent and the buyer have done what the judge does, then just as the judge's returning the slave to the agent results in return to the principal, similarly, without a judicial decision, merely by the agent's acknowledgment of the return of the slave to the agent, it will also become returned to the principal. The agent will not need litigation to return it to the principal. And in the general narrations of al-Mabsut, it is that in this case the slave is returned only to the agent and not to the principal, and the agent does not have the right to litigate with his principal. That is, this return is binding upon the agent. The agent himself will return the price to the buyer, and the agent himself will become the owner of the slave, and the agent will not have the right to bring litigation before the judge to return the slave to his principal.

Its proof has passed earlier: the return of the slave without a judicial decision due to acknowledgment, although it is an exchanged sale (masakh bay') between the agent and the buyer, it is a new sale (bay' jadid) for others. And the principal is other than these two. So why should the sale concluded between these two be binding upon the other, i.e., the principal? Therefore, this slave will not be binding upon the principal.

Sahib al-Hidayah, responding to 'because the return is determined (li-anna al-radda muta'ayyin)', said: Your statement that the return of the slave is determined is incorrect. Because the buyer's right is first established in the description of soundness. That is, the buyer is entitled to a sound and intact sale. But when the sold item is not sound but has a defect, his right transfers to returning the sold item. That is, the buyer gains the right to return the sold item. However, if besides the old defect, a new defect has arisen in the sold item while in the buyer's possession, or the old defect has increased, then in that case the buyer does not have the right to return the sold item. Rather, the buyer can recover the amount of his loss from the price. That is, the decrease in the slave's value caused by the defect that existed at the seller's end, the buyer is authorized to take that amount from the seller. In any case, it is established that returning the sold item due to a defect is not determined, and when returning is not determined, your stated proof of 'because the return is determined' is also not correct.

Sahib al-Hidayah says that this issue is explained in more detail in al-Kifayah.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the return of a defective slave sold through an agent. The issue: if an agent acknowledges a defect without litigation, does the slave automatically return to the principal? One narration from al-Mabsut (Kitab al-Buyu) says yes, because the defect is such that it could not have originated with the buyer, so the return is 'determined' (muta'ayyin) – the agent and buyer's act mimics the judge's ruling. The general narrations of al-Mabsut say no: the slave returns only to the agent, who becomes owner and must compensate the buyer, and the agent cannot litigate against the principal. The author then clarifies the reasoning: the agent's acknowledgment creates a new sale (bay' jadid) for third parties like the principal, so it cannot bind him. Sahib al-Hidayah refutes the 'determined return' argument by explaining that return for defect is not automatically determined; it is subject to conditions (e.g., no new defect). Therefore, the proof based on 'determined return' fails. The author notes that al-Kifayah provides further detail.

Key Terms
متعین (muta'ayyin): Determined or automatic; in this context, the return of the defective item is considered inevitable without need for litigation.
مسخ بیج (masakh bay'): A sale that is exchanged; here referring to the return transaction between agent and buyer being an exchange sale valid between them.
بیج جدید (bay' jadid): A new sale; the return is considered a new contract of sale when viewed from the perspective of outsiders like the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general narration of al-Mabsut, what is the effect of the agent's acknowledgment of a defect in a sold slave without litigation?
A) The slave returns to the principal.
B) The slave returns only to the agent, and the agent becomes the owner.
C) The buyer keeps the slave and gets a price reduction.
D) The slave is returned to the original seller (not the principal).

Key Takeaway
In an agency sale, an agent's acknowledgment of a defect without litigation does not bind the principal; the slave returns only to the agent per the relied-upon narration.

Dispute About Cash vs. Credit Sale: Principal's Word is Accepted
Ruling that principal's claim of condition is taken over agent's claim of absolute authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on disputes between principal and agent regarding the nature of a sale (cash vs. credit) and explain why the principal's word is accepted.

Core Idea
In a dispute over agency conditions, the principal's word prevails because he is the source of the command.

When a principal and agent disagree about whether a sale was restricted to cash or left absolute, who should a judge believe? The Hanafi school gives priority to the principal, relying on the principle that the originator of the command knows its content best. This lesson explains the ruling and its rationale from the Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
The principal claims he specified cash; the agent claims the command was absolute (no restriction). Recognize that the dispute is about the content of the original command.
The scenario assumes no external evidence (qarina) for either side.
2
Locate the source of authority
The command originates from the principal. He is the one who issued the instruction, so he knows best what he said.
This is a logical (aqli) proof: the speaker has privileged knowledge of his own utterance.
3
Apply the principle of credibility
In the absence of proof to the contrary, the principal's statement about his own command is taken as true. The agent, being merely the executor, cannot overrule the principal's account.
The default is that the command is as the principal claims; reliability of the agent does not affect this.
4
Check for evidence of absoluteness
The text states there is no indication (dalalah) that the command was absolute. Thus, the command cannot be presumed absolute.
If there were a qarina (contextual evidence), the ruling might differ.
5
Derive the practical outcome
Since the principal's word is accepted, the sale is treated as a cash sale. This affects the validity and obligations of the transaction.
If the agent sold on credit, that sale may be void or subject to the principal's ratification.

Source Text

ایک آدمی نے دوسرے سے کہا میں نے تجھ کو اپنا غلام نقد کے عوض فروخت کرنے کا حکم کیا لیکن تم نے اسکو ادھار کے عوض بیچا اور وکیل نے کہا کہ تو نے مجھ کو اسکے بیچنے کا حکم کیا تھا اور کچھ نہیں کہا تو مؤکل کا قول معتبر ہوگا
قال ومن قال لأخر امرتك ببيع عبــــدى بنقـــــد فيعتـــــه بنسيئه وقــــال المامور امرتني ببيعه ولم تقل شيئاً فالقول قول الأمر لان الامر يستفاد من جهته ولا دلالة على الاطلاق 
ترجمہ اگر ایک آدمی نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ میں نے تجھ کو اپنا غلام نفقد کے عوض فروخت کرنے کا حکم کیا تھا۔ لیکن تو نے اس کو ادھار کے عوض بیچا ہے اور وکیل نے کہا کہ تو نے مجھ کو اس کے بیچنے کا حکم کیا تھا اور کچھ نہیں کہا تھا تو موکل کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اسلئے کہ حکم اسی کی طرف سے مستفاد ہے اور اطلاق پر کوئی دلالت نہیں ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مؤکل اور وکیل کے درمیان اختلاف ہوا اور موکل نے کہا کہ میں نے تجھ کو نقد کے عوض بیچنے کا وکیل کیا تھا۔ لیکن تو نے غلام کو ادھا کے عوض فروخت کیا ہے۔ اور وکیل نے کہا کہ ایسا نہیں بلکہ آپ نے تصرف کو مطلق رکھا تھا۔ یعنی صرف غلام بیچنے کا وکیل کیا تھا اور اس سے زائد کچھ نہیں کہا تھا نہ نقد کے عوض بیچنے کیلئے کہا تھا اور نہ ادھار کے عوض تو اس صورت میں مؤکل کا قول معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ امر مؤکل کی طرف سے مستفاد ہوتا ہے اور جس کی طرف سے امر مستفاد ہوتا ہے وہ اپنے قول سے خوب واقف ہوتا ہے۔ الہد اسی کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اور یہاں امر بالمیچ کے مطلق ہونے پر چونکہ کوئی دلیل اور قرینہ بھی موجود نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے بھی اس امر بالبیع کو طلاق پر محمول نہیں کیا جائے گا۔
A person said to another: 'I commanded you to sell my slave for cash, but you sold him on credit.' The agent said: 'You commanded me to sell him and did not say anything [about conditions].' Then the principal's word is accepted.

He (the author) said: And whoever says to another: 'I commanded you to sell my slave for cash, and you sold him on credit,' and the commanded one says: 'You commanded me to sell him and did not say anything,' then the word is the word of the commander (principal), because the command is derived from him and there is no indication of absolute [authority].

Translation: If a person said to another: 'I commanded you to sell my slave for cash, but you sold him on credit,' and the agent said: 'You commanded me to sell him and did not say anything,' then the principal's statement is accepted. Because the command is derived from him, and there is no indication of absolute authority. Explanation: The scenario is that if a dispute arises between principal and agent, and the principal says: 'I appointed you to sell for cash, but you sold the slave on credit,' and the agent says: 'No, rather you left the disposition absolute, i.e., you only appointed me to sell the slave and said nothing more—you did not say to sell for cash nor for credit,' then the principal's statement is accepted because the command is derived from the principal, and the one from whom the command is derived is well aware of his own statement. Therefore, his statement is accepted. And here, since there is no evidence or context indicating that the command to sell was absolute, this command to sell cannot be considered absolute.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on a dispute between principal and agent regarding whether the sale was restricted to cash or credit. The matn states that when the principal claims he specified cash and the agent claims the command was absolute, the principal's word is taken. The proof: the command originates from the principal, so he knows best what he said. Moreover, there is no external evidence (qarina) indicating absolute authority. The commentary explains the scenario: the principal says he restricted to cash; the agent says no restriction. Since the principal is the source of the command and there is no evidence of absoluteness, the default is that the command is restricted as the principal claims. This is a classic Hanafi principle: in cases of conflicting claims, the one who originates the command is believed in the absence of proof to the contrary.

Key Terms
نقد (naqd): Cash; immediate payment in the sale transaction.
نسيئة (nasi'ah): Credit; deferred payment in a sale.
اطلاق (itlaq): Absolute or unrestricted; the command is general without qualification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the principal claims he restricted a sale to cash and the agent claims the command was absolute, whose statement is accepted according to the Hanafi school?
A) The agent's, because he is the one who sold.
B) The principal's, because the command originates from him.
C) Both equally; a judge must investigate.
D) Neither; the buyer's statement is accepted.

Key Takeaway
In a dispute between principal and agent about the terms of a sale, the principal's word is accepted because he is the originator of the command and there is no presumption of absolute authority.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Dispute between Principal and Agent on Cash vs Credit Sale
Ruling on whose word is taken when principal and agent disagree about cash or credit sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose word is taken when a principal and agent disagree about cash vs credit sale, and explain the rational proof (dalil aqli) that the principal's statement is preferred.

Core Idea
When principal and agent differ on sale terms, the principal's word prevails because he knows his own command.

In the chapter of agency (wakalah), disputes often arise about the scope of the agent's authority. This lesson examines a classic scenario: the principal claims he ordered a cash sale, while the agent insists the command was absolute. The Hanafi school provides a clear procedural ruling based on the origin of the mandate. By understanding the proof, you will grasp how the school prioritizes the principal's knowledge of his own intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The principal says: 'I ordered you to sell for cash.' The agent says: 'You ordered me to sell, without specifying cash or credit.' Note that both agree an agency existed; the disagreement is over the restriction.
No evidence (bayyinah) is presented by either side; this is a pure claim dispute.
2
State the ruling
The text states: 'The principal's statement is credible' (f al-qawlu qawlu al-amir). The principal's word is taken over the agent's.
This is a procedural rule of evidence, not a hukm taklifi.
3
Examine the rational proof
The proof is: 'because the command is derived from him (the principal) and there is no indication of absoluteness' (lianna al-amra yustafadu min jihatihi wa la dalala 'ala al-itlaq). The principal is the source of the mandate; he knows best what he intended. The agent's claim of itlaq (absoluteness) lacks any supporting evidence.
The burden of proof is on the one claiming a broader authority (the agent).
4
Understand the implication of 'itlaq'
'Itlaq' means the command was unrestricted. In Hanafi law, if a principal gives an absolute command to sell, the agent may sell by cash or credit at his discretion. However, in this dispute, the principal denies giving such broad authority. Since there is no external evidence (qarina) that the command was absolute, the default is to accept the principal's restricted version.
This aligns with the principle that the one who originates a statement is the best interpreter of it.
5
Contrast with cases where evidence exists
If the agent can produce evidence (e.g., witnesses or a written document) that the principal's command was absolute, the ruling may shift. But in the absence of such proof, the principal's word stands.
The commentary from Al-Hidayah confirms this position.

Source Text

ایک آدمی نے دوسرے سے کہا میں نے تجھ کو اپنا غلام نقد کے عوض فروخت کرنے کا حکم کیا لیکن تم نے اسکو ادھار کے عوض بیچا اور وکیل نے کہا کہ تو نے مجھ کو اسکے بیچنے کا حکم کیا تھا اور کچھ نہیں کہا تو مؤکل کا قول معتبر ہوگا
قال ومن قال لأخر امرتك ببيع عبــــدى بنقـــــد فيعتـــــه بنسيئه وقــــال المامور امرتني ببيعه ولم تقل شيئاً فالقول قول الأمر لان الامر يستفاد من جهته ولا دلالة على الاطلاق ترجمہ اگر ایک آدمی نے دوسرے سے کہا کہ میں نے تجھ کو اپنا غلام نفقد کے عوض فروخت کرنے کا حکم کیا تھا۔ لیکن تو نے اس کو ادھار کے عوض بیچا ہے اور وکیل نے کہا کہ تو نے مجھ کو اس کے بیچنے کا حکم کیا تھا اور کچھ نہیں کہا تھا تو موکل کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اسلئے کہ حکم اسی کی طرف سے مستفاد ہے اور اطلاق پر کوئی دلالت نہیں ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر مؤکل اور وکیل کے درمیان اختلاف ہوا اور موکل نے کہا کہ میں نے تجھ کو نقد کے عوض بیچنے کا وکیل کیا تھا۔ لیکن تو نے غلام کو ادھا کے عوض فروخت کیا ہے۔ اور وکیل نے کہا کہ ایسا نہیں بلکہ آپ نے تصرف کو مطلق رکھا تھا۔ یعنی صرف غلام بیچنے کا وکیل کیا تھا اور اس سے زائد کچھ نہیں کہا تھا نہ نقد کے عوض بیچنے کیلئے کہا تھا اور نہ ادھار کے عوض تو اس صورت میں مؤکل کا قول معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ امر مؤکل کی طرف سے مستفاد ہوتا ہے اور جس کی طرف سے امر مستفاد ہوتا ہے وہ اپنے قول سے خوب واقف ہوتا ہے۔ الہد اسی کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اور یہاں امر بالمیچ کے مطلق ہونے پر چونکہ کوئی دلیل اور قرینہ بھی موجود نہیں ہے۔ اسلئے بھی اس امر بالبیع کو طلاق پر محمول نہیں کیا جائے گا۔
A man said to another: I commanded you to sell my slave for cash, but you sold him on credit. And the agent said: You commanded me to sell him and did not say anything (about payment). Then the principal's statement is credible because the command is derived from him and there is no indication of absoluteness. Translation: If a man said to another: I commanded you to sell my slave for cash, but you sold him on credit, and the agent said: You commanded me to sell him and did not say anything (about payment). Then the principal's statement is credible because the command is derived from him and there is no indication of absoluteness. Explanation: The scenario is that if the principal and agent differ, and the principal says: I appointed you as agent to sell for cash, but you sold the slave on credit. And the agent says: No, rather you left the transaction absolute, i.e., you only appointed me as agent to sell the slave and said nothing more, neither for cash nor for credit. In this case, the principal's statement is credible because the command is derived from the principal, and the one from whom the command is derived knows his own statement best. In Al-Hidayah, his statement is credible. And here, since there is no evidence or indication that the command to sell was absolute, this command to sell is not interpreted as absolute.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter of agency (wakalah): When the principal and agent disagree about the terms of a sale—specifically whether it was to be for cash or credit—the principal's word is taken over the agent's. The proof is twofold: first, the command originates from the principal, so he knows best what he intended; second, there is no external indication that the command was absolute (i.e., allowing both cash and credit). Therefore, the command is interpreted as restricted to what the principal claims. The commentary (sharh) explains that in Al-Hidayah, the principal's statement is accepted because he is the source of the mandate. This ruling applies when there is no evidence (bayyinah) from either side.

Key Terms
موکل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent (wakil).
وکیل (wakil): The agent appointed by the principal to perform an act.
بیع نقد (bay' naqd): A sale for immediate payment (cash).
بیع نسیئہ (bay' nasi'ah): A sale on credit with deferred payment.
اطلاق (itlaq): Absoluteness or generality of a command without restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a principal says he appointed the agent to sell for cash, but the agent claims the appointment was absolute (no restriction), whose statement is taken?
A) The agent's
B) The principal's
C) Both are equal
D) The buyer's

Key Takeaway
In disputes between principal and agent regarding the terms of sale, the Hanafi school accepts the principal's word due to the origin of the command and the absence of proof of absoluteness.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Agent for Sale: No Liability for Lost Security
Ruling on agent liability for lost security

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that an agent for sale is not liable when a pledge or guarantor taken for the price is lost, and distinguish this from an agent for debt collection.

Core Idea
The agent for sale is asil (principal) in rights; taking security is his own right, not a trust.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses the liability of an agent for sale (wakil bil-bay') when he takes a pledge or guarantor for the price and that security is subsequently lost. The ruling may seem counterintuitive: one might expect the agent to be liable for loss of property in his possession. However, the Hanafi school distinguishes between agents who act as principals (asil) in contractual rights and those who act purely as deputies (na'ib). Understanding this distinction is key to applying the correct ruling in agency transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? The agent for sale takes a pledge (rahn) or guarantor (kafil) for the price, and the security is lost. The ruling: the agent is not liable (la daman).
The ruling is about the agent's liability, not the validity of the sale.
2
Locate the dalil (legal reasoning)
The author provides the ta'lil (reason): 'because the agent is principal in the rights (asil fil-huquq).' This is the key proof. The agent's possession of the price is a right of the sale contract, and taking security is a reinforcement of that right.
The dalil is qiyas: the agent for sale is analogized to an owner, not a trustee.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the reasoning yield the ruling? If the agent were a trustee (like a debt collector), his possession would be a trust (amanah), and loss would require compensation. But because he is asil, his possession is like an owner's – loss is his own risk. The text confirms: 'and for this reason the principal does not have the power to prevent him from it.'
The distinction hinges on whether the agent is asil or na'ib.
4
Note the ikhtilaf with Imam Malik
The commentary mentions a scenario where a qadi rules according to Imam Malik's view that the original debtor is discharged by the guarantee. This does not affect the Hanafi ruling; the agent remains not liable.
The ikhtilaf is about the effect of guarantee, not about the agent's liability.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab distinction
Compare the agent for sale with an agent for debt collection. The latter acts as na'ib, so taking security beyond his authority incurs liability. The thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in who bears loss.
This distinction is essential for correct fatwa.

کو مضبوط ہی کرتے ہیں۔ پس جب ثمن وصول کرنا وکیل کا حق ہے تو کفیل لینا اور رہن لینا جو اس حق کو موکد کرتے ہیں وہ بھی وکیل کے حقوق ہوں گے۔ اور وکیل ہی ان کا مالک ہوگا اور وکیل کے قبضہ سے اگر ثمن ہلاک ہو جاتا تو وکیل پر اسکا ضمان واجب نہ ہوتا۔ کیونکہ ثمن پر وکیل کا قبضہ، قبضہ امانت ہوتا ہے اور قبضہ امانت ہلاک ہونے کی وجہ سے ضمان واجب نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا وکیل پر ثمن کا ضمان بھی واجب نہ ہو گا۔ پس رہن نائب کیا ہے اور کفیل لینے کا اور رہن لینے کا چونکہ اس کو وکیل اور نائب نہیں کیا ہے۔ اسلئے وہ قرضدار سے کفیل لینے اور رہن لینے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا۔ اور وکیل بالبیع چونکہ حقوق بیع میں اصیل ہوتا ہے۔ اسلئے وہ ثمن پر اصالتہ قبضہ کرتا ہے۔ نہ کہ نیابتہ یہی وجہ ہے کہ مؤکل اس کو ثمن پر قبضہ کرنے سے روکنے کا اختیار نہیں رکھتا ہے بلکہ وکیل اس صورت میں مالک کے مرتبہ میں ہوگا اور مالک اگر ثمن کے عوض رہن لے لے یا ثمن کے واسطے کفیل لے لے تو جائز ہے۔ لہذا وکیل بالبیع بھی رہن لینے اور کفیل لینے کا مجاز ہوگا۔
He said: Whoever instructs a man to sell his slave, and he sells him and takes a pledge for the price, and it is lost in his hand, or takes a guarantor for it, then the money is lost upon him, so there is no liability upon him because the agent is principal in the rights, and taking possession of the price is among them, and the guarantor is a strengthening for it, and taking a pledge is a security for the side of collection, so he owns them. Unlike the agent for collecting a debt, because he acts by representation, and he appointed him for taking possession of the debt, not for the guarantor and taking. And the agent for sale takes possession as a principal, and therefore the principal does not have the power to prevent him from it. Translation: If a man instructs another to sell his slave, then the agent sells the slave and takes a pledge for the price, and the pledged property is lost in his possession, or he takes a guarantor for the price, then the money is lost upon him, so the agent is not liable. Because in the rights of sale, the agent is a principal. And taking possession of the price is among the rights. And taking a guarantor is strengthening for the price, and taking a pledge is also a security for the side of collection. So the agent owns both. Unlike the agent for collecting a debt, because he acts by representation, and the principal appointed him to take possession of the debt, not to accept guarantee or take pledge. And the agent for sale takes possession as a principal; for this reason the principal does not have the authority to prevent him from taking possession of the price. Explanation: The scenario is that a man appointed another as agent to sell his slave. Then the agent sold the slave and took a pledge from the buyer for the price, and that pledged property was lost in his possession. Or the agent took a guarantor from the buyer for the price, but the money was lost upon the guarantor, such that both the guarantor and the guaranteed (i.e., the buyer) died insolvent, or the guarantor died and the guaranteed disappeared so that his whereabouts could not be known, or the guarantor died and the guaranteed is alive but when the case came before the qadi, the qadi, according to the madhhab of Imam Malik, holds that the original debtor is discharged by the guarantee itself. So he ruled the discharge of the original debtor according to his belief and madhhab. In these three cases, the money that was owed as the price upon the buyer is lost, i.e., collection of the price becomes impossible. Nevertheless, in both the pledge and guarantee cases, no liability falls upon the agent towards the principal. Because the agent is principal in the contractual rights, i.e., the monetary rights essentially revert to the agent, and taking possession of the price is among the contractual rights, and taking a guarantor and taking a pledge from the buyer to collect the price are securities and strengthenings for the side of collection. So when collecting the price is the agent's right, then taking the guarantor and pledge which reinforce that right are also the agent's rights. And the agent owns them. And if the price were lost in the agent's possession, he would not be liable for it, because the agent's possession of the price is a possessory trust, and a possessory trust does not incur liability due to loss. Therefore, the agent is not liable for the price. Since the pledge is a substitute and representative of the price, he is not liable for its loss either. And in the case of guarantee, he is also not liable, because in this case the loss of the price itself has occurred, and when the price is lost, the agent is not liable. So in this case too, there is no liability. In contrast, if a person appointed someone as agent to collect his debt, and then the agent took a pledge from the debtor for the debt, then this taking of pledge and guarantor by the agent is not permissible. So if that pledge is lost in the agent's possession, or the guarantor and the guaranteed (debtor) both died insolvent, the agent is not absolved from liability; rather, he is liable for the debt. Because the agent for debt collection collects the debt as a deputy of the principal, so he is only authorized to collect the principal's debt, because the principal appointed him only as a deputy to collect the debt, and since he was not made agent or deputy to take a guarantor or pledge, he is not authorized to take a guarantor or pledge from the debtor. And the agent for sale, since he is principal in the rights of sale, he takes possession of the price as a principal, not as a deputy. For this reason, the principal does not have the authority to prevent him from taking possession of the price; rather, the agent is in the position of an owner, and if an owner takes a pledge for the price or takes a guarantor for the price, it is permissible. Therefore, the agent for sale is also authorized to take a pledge and a guarantor.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This is a ruling on the liability of an agent for sale (wakil bil-bay') who takes security for the price. The passage begins by stating the ruling: if an agent sells and takes a pledge (rahn) or a guarantor (kafil) for the price, and that security is subsequently lost, the agent is not liable to the principal. The reason is that the agent for sale acts as a principal (asil) in the rights of the contract, including taking possession of the price. Taking a pledge or guarantor is a means of strengthening and securing the collection of the price, which is the agent's own right. Therefore, the agent owns these securities. If the price itself were lost in his possession, he would not be liable because his possession is a trust (amanah). Similarly, the loss of the pledge or the loss of the price due to the guarantor's default does not create liability. The passage then contrasts this with the case of an agent appointed to collect a debt (wakil bi-qabd al-dayn). Such an agent acts purely as a deputy (na'ib); he only has authority to collect the debt, not to take pledges or guarantors. If he does so and the security is lost, he is liable because he exceeded his authority. The key distinction is that the agent for sale is considered an 'asil (principal) in the rights of the sale, whereas the agent for debt collection remains a deputy. This is evidenced by the fact that the principal cannot prevent the agent for sale from taking possession of the price, while the principal can restrict the debt collector. The commentary elaborates on three specific scenarios where the security is lost: death of both parties insolvent, disappearance, or a qadi's ruling discharging the debtor based on Imam Malik's position. In all cases, the agent is not liable. The lesson is crucial for understanding the difference in authority between different types of agents in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالبيع (al-wakīl bil-bay‘): An agent appointed to sell a specific item; in this text, he is considered asil (principal) in the rights of sale.
اصيل (aṣīl): A principal who acts on his own behalf, not as a deputy; the agent for sale is asil in rights of the sale contract.
وثيقة (wathīqah): A security or collateral, such as a pledge (rahn) or guarantor (kafalah), that strengthens the right of collection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is an agent for sale (wakil bil-bay') not liable when a pledge taken for the price is lost in his possession?
A) Because the pledge is the principal's property and the agent is a trustee.
B) Because the agent acts as a principal (asil) in the rights of sale, so taking a pledge is his own right, not a trust.
C) Because the agent has no authority to take a pledge, so it is void.
D) Because the principal must bear all losses in agency.

Key Takeaway
An agent for sale is asil in rights; taking security is his own right, so loss of security does not incur liability.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Split Acceptance of Bayyinah Against an Agent for Possession
Ruling on acceptance of possessor's bayyinah to obstruct wakil's possession over a specific item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between a wakil bi-qabd al-'ayn and a wakil bi-al-khusuma to determine when a possessor's bayyinah is accepted in court.

Core Idea
A possessor's bayyinah against an agent is only accepted to challenge the agent's authority, not to prove ownership — a split acceptance by istihsan.

In this lesson, we examine a classic Hanafi case where a wakil (agent) is sent to take possession of an item, but the current possessor claims he owns it. The question is whether the possessor's evidence (bayyinah) can block the agent. The Hanafi school distinguishes two aspects of the evidence: one concerning the agent's authority (where the agent is a litigant) and one concerning the sale itself (where he is not). This ruling, by istihsan, leads to a suspension rather than outright dismissal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
Recognise the principal (who appointed the agent), the agent (wakil bi-qabd al-'ayn), and the possessor (who claims ownership). The dispute is whether the possessor's bayyinah can prevent the agent from taking the item.
The agent is only authorised to take possession, not to litigate.
2
State the ruling by qiyas
According to strict analogy, the bayyinah should be completely rejected because the agent is not a litigant (khasm). The agent should be given possession immediately.
Qiyas treats the agent as a mere trustee, not a party to any ownership claim.
3
State the ruling by istihsan
By juristic preference, the matter is suspended. The bayyinah is accepted only to show that the agent's authority is defective (taqsir al-qabd), but not to prove the sale itself.
Istihsan splits the bayyinah into two distinct claims.
4
Analyse the split: why the bayyinah is partially accepted
The possessor's bayyinah contains two claims: (1) a sale from the principal, and (2) a defect in the agent's authority. For claim (1), the agent is not a litigant, so it is not accepted. For claim (2), the agent is a litigant (his right to take is challenged), so it is accepted. Thus, the agent cannot take the item until the principal returns.
The agent becomes a litigant only regarding his own authority, not the underlying transaction.
5
Extend the ruling to analogous cases
The same principle applies to divorce (talaq) and manumission ('itaq): if an agent is sent to bring a wife or a slave, and the wife or slave produces bayyinah claiming divorce or freedom, it is accepted only to block the agent's possession, not to establish the divorce or freedom itself.
The principal must be present for the substantive claim to be proven.

تشریح صاحب جامع صغیر نے کہا اگر کسی شخص نے اپنی بیوی لانے کے واسطے کسی کو وکیل بنا کر بھیجا۔ یا کسی نے اپنا غلام یا با ندی لانے کے واسطے کسی کو وکیل بنا کر بھیجا پس جب وکیل نے اپنے مؤکل کی بیوی کو لانے کا ارادہ کیا تو اس نے بینہ قائم کیا کہ میرا شوہر یعنی اس کا مؤکل مجھے کو طلاق دے چکا ہے یا غلام یا باندی نے بینہ قائم کیا کہ میرا آقا یعنی اس کا مؤکل مجھ کو آزاد کر چکا ہے تو استحسانا یہ بینہ وکیل کے قبضہ کو کوتاہ کرنے میں قبول کر لیا جائے گا یعنی اس بینہ کی وجہ سے وکیل مؤکل کی بیوی یا غلام یا باندی پر قبضہ کرنے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا لیکن یہ بعینہ طلاق اور عتناق کے حق میں قبول نہ ہوگا یعنی اس بینہ کی وجہ سے نہ عورت پر طلاق واقع ہوگی اور نہ غلام اور باندی آزاد ہوں گے ہاں اگر مؤکل جو غائب تھا حاضر ہو گیا تو طلاق اور عتاق ثابت کرنے کیلئے اس کی موجودگی میں دوبارہ بیٹہ پیش کرنا ضروری ہوگا۔سابقہ بینہ کافی نہ ہوگا۔
Book of Agency. Ashraf al-Hidaya Volume Ten. So that if the absent one returns, the bayyinah on the sale will be repeated. So it becomes like when the possessor established bayyinah that the principal had dismissed this agent from taking possession. Therefore, it will be accepted regarding the defectiveness of his possession. Similarly, testimony will be accepted in this place. Explanation: The case is that if someone appointed an agent to take possession of a specific thing, this agent is not an agent for litigation. The proof is that in this case the agent is merely a trustee, and since the agent takes possession on behalf of the principal, this possession is not a transactional possession. As in the previous case, possession of the specific thing is not transactional. So this possession does not necessitate transactional rights, and when rights are not established through this possession, the agent is not a litigant. Rather, in this case the agent's status is merely that of a trustee and attaché. Hence, if someone appointed an agent to take possession of his slave, and the one in possession of the slave established bayyinah that the principal had sold this slave to him, then this matter is suspended until the absent principal comes. That is, the agent will neither be given possession nor will possession be refused. And this ruling is by istihsan. But the requirement of qiyas is that the slave should be handed over to the agent, and the possessor's bayyinah should not be considered. The reason is that the agent for possession of the 'ayn is not an agent for litigation, so here the agent is not a litigant. And when the agent is not a litigant, the possessor's bayyinah is not against any litigant or defendant, and a bayyinah that is not established against a litigant is not considered. Therefore, this bayyinah of the possessor is not considered. And when the possessor's bayyinah is not considered, the agent's possession of the slave cannot be prevented; rather, the agent should be given possession of the slave. The reason for istihsan is that the possessor's bayyinah is about two things: one is that the principal sold this slave to the possessor, so the principal's ownership of the slave is removed; second is that the agent's possession has become defective, i.e., the agent is not authorized to take possession of this slave. In the first matter, the agent is clearly not the possessor's litigant. But in the second matter, the agent is the possessor's litigant. So in the first matter, where the agent is not a litigant, the possessor's bayyinah is not accepted, meaning the possessor's purchase from the principal is not proven and the principal's ownership of the slave is not removed. But in the second matter, where the agent is a litigant, the possessor's bayyinah is accepted, and as a result the agent's possession becomes defective. That is, the agent is not authorized to take possession of the slave. So that if the absent principal comes, it is necessary for the possessor to establish bayyinah again in the presence of the principal that he bought the slave from the principal. And for proving the sale, the previous bayyinah that was established in the absence of the principal will not be accepted because the agent was not the possessor's litigant in this regard. Therefore, the bayyinah presented by the possessor against the agent in this regard is not reliable. And when the previous bayyinah is not reliable, it is necessary for the possessor to present bayyinah again to prove the sale. This case becomes like when the possessor established bayyinah that the principal had dismissed the agent from taking possession of the slave; this bayyinah is accepted regarding making the agent's possession defective. Similarly, in the mentioned case, the possessor's bayyinah is accepted only for making the agent's possession defective, and not for proving the sale. The above ruling also applies to divorce and manumission. He said: And likewise manumission, divorce, and other than that. Its meaning: if a woman establishes bayyinah of divorce, and a male slave or female slave establishes bayyinah of manumission against the agent who came to take them, it is accepted for the purpose of making the agent's possession defective until the absent one comes, by istihsan, not for the manumission and divorce. Translation: And the same ruling applies to manumission, divorce, etc. Its meaning: if a woman established bayyinah of divorce, and a male slave or female slave established bayyinah of manumission before that agent who came to take them, then by istihsan this bayyinah is accepted in making the agent's possession defective until the absent one appears, not regarding the manumission and divorce. Explanation: The author of al-Jami' al-Saghir said: If a person sent an agent to bring his wife, or someone sent an agent to bring his male or female slave, then when the agent intended to take possession of his principal's wife, she established bayyinah that her husband (i.e., the principal) has divorced her, or the male or female slave established bayyinah that their master (i.e., the principal) has freed them, then by istihsan this bayyinah is accepted in making the agent's possession defective, i.e., due to this bayyinah, the agent is not authorized to take possession of the principal's wife or slave. But this bayyinah is not accepted regarding the divorce and manumission itself, i.e., due to this bayyinah, neither does divorce occur on the woman, nor does the slave become free. Yes, if the principal who was absent becomes present, then to prove the divorce and manumission, it is necessary to present bayyinah again in his presence; the previous bayyinah will not be sufficient.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
Passage type: Ruling with proof (istihsan vs qiyas) concerning the acceptance of bayyinah presented by a possessor against a wakil appointed for taking possession of a specific item (wakil bi-qabd al-'ayn). The author first states the case: Zayd appoints 'Amr as his agent to take possession of a slave currently in Bakr's possession. Bakr claims he bought the slave from Zayd and presents two witnesses to prove the sale. According to qiyas (analogy), the agent should be given the slave because the wakil bi-qabd is not a litigation agent (wakil bi-al-khusuma), so Bakr's bayyinah is not against a proper adversary and thus should be ignored. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), the matter is suspended: the agent is neither given possession nor denied it outright. The reason is that Bakr's bayyinah actually contains two distinct claims: (1) that Zayd sold him the slave, and (2) that the agent's authority to take possession is defective. Regarding the first claim, the agent is not a litigant because he is only authorized to take possession, not to defend ownership claims. Therefore, that part of the bayyinah is not accepted—the sale is not proven, and Zayd's ownership remains intact. Regarding the second claim, the agent is indeed a litigant because the possessor is challenging the agent's right to take possession. Thus, the bayyinah is accepted to the extent that it obstructs the agent's possession: the agent cannot take the slave until Zayd returns. When Zayd does return, Bakr must present a new bayyinah for the sale in Zayd's presence because the previous bayyinah regarding the sale was not effective. The author then extends this ruling to analogous cases of divorce (talaq) and manumission ('itaq): if a husband sends an agent to bring his wife, and the wife claims before the agent that the husband divorced her, or if a master sends an agent to bring a slave and the slave claims manumission, the bayyinah is accepted only to prevent the agent from taking them, not to legally establish the divorce or manumission. Those must be re-proven in the principal's presence. This analytical distinction between the two aspects of the bayyinah is the key contribution of istihsan over qiyas.

Key Terms
وکیل بالقبض (wakil bi al-qabd): An agent authorized only to take physical possession of a specific item, not to engage in litigation or legal claims.
بینہ (bayyinah): Legal evidence, typically that of two male witnesses or one male and two females, presented to establish a claim in court.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable outcome due to a specific legal reason (dalil).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a wakil bi-qabd al-'ayn comes to take possession of a slave, and the possessor presents bayyinah claiming he bought the slave from the principal, what is the Hanafi ruling by istihsan?
A) The bayyinah is completely accepted, and the slave is given to the possessor.
B) The bayyinah is completely rejected, and the slave is given to the wakil.
C) The matter is suspended until the principal returns; the bayyinah is accepted only to obstruct the wakil's possession, not to prove the sale.
D) The wakil is required to return to the principal to confirm the sale.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a possessor's bayyinah against a wakil bi-qabd al-'ayn is partially accepted: it can block the agent's possession (where the agent is a litigant) but not prove the underlying claim of ownership (where the agent is not a litigant).

Purchase Claim on Deposit: Depositee Not to Hand Over While Depositor Lives
Ruling on claim of having bought the deposit while depositor is alive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer claims to have purchased a deposited item from the depositor and the depositee confirms, and explain the legal reasoning that the depositor's ongoing ownership prevents the handover.

Core Idea
A trustee's confirmation of a sale is invalid while the trustor lives because ownership remains with the trustor.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific situation: a person claims to have bought a deposited item from the depositor (the owner), and the depositee (the trustee) confirms the claim. The ruling is that the depositee is not ordered to hand over the item. We will examine why this is so, focusing on the principle that the depositor's ownership continues during his life and that the depositee's acknowledgment cannot affect that ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
Recognize that there are three parties: the depositor (owner), the depositee (trustee), and the claimant (buyer). The buyer claims to have bought the deposit from the depositor. The depositee confirms the claim.
2
State the ruling
According to the text, the judge will not order the depositee to hand over the deposit to the buyer.
3
Analyze the first reason
The depositor is alive and capable of ownership, so the deposit remains his property. The buyer's claim is unproven against the depositor.
4
Analyze the second reason
The depositee's confirmation is an acknowledgment of the buyer's right over property that belongs to another. In Hanafi law, such an acknowledgment is invalid because it affects a third party's rights without their consent.
5
Note the practical implication
This prevents collusion between the depositee and a third party to deprive the depositor of his property.
The ruling differs if the depositor is deceased; then the deposit becomes part of the estate and the claimant may have stronger grounds.

صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ اگر ایک شخص نے دعوی کیا کہ میں نے مودع ( بكسر الدال) سے مال ودیعت خرید لیا ہے اور مودَع ( فتح الدال) نے مدعی شراء کی تصدیق بھی کر دی تو مودع ( فتح الدال) کو یہ حکم نہ دیا جائے گا کہ مال ودیعت کو مدعی شراء کے سپرد کر دے کیونکہ مودع ( بكسر الدال ) جب تک زندہ ہے تب تک وہ مالک ہونے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے لہذا مال ودیعت کا مودع ( بكسر الدال ) ہی مالک ہے۔ اور جب مودع ( بكسر الدال ) مال ودیعت کا مالک ہے تو مودع ( بفتح الدال ) کا مدعی شراء کے قول کی تصدیق کرنا غیر یعنی مودع ( بكسر الدال ) کے مال کا مدعی شراء کیلئے اقرار کرنا ہوا اور غیر کے مال کا کسی دوسرے کیلئے اقرار کرنا چونکہ مودع ( بكسر الدال ) کے خلاف مودع ( بفتح الدال ) اور مدعی شراء کے قول کی تصدیق نہ کی جائے گی ۔ اور جب ان دونوں کے قول کی تصدیق نہیں کی گئی تو مودع ( بفتح الدال ) کو یہ حکم بھی نہ دیا جائیگا کہ وہ مدعی شراء کو مال ودیعت دیدے۔
The author of Al-Hidayah stated: If a person claimed, 'I have bought the deposit property from the depositor (mūdi‘),' and the depositee (mūda‘) confirmed the buyer claimant's statement, then the depositee will not be ordered to hand over the deposit property to the buyer claimant, because as long as the depositor (mūdi‘) is alive, he is capable of ownership, so the deposit property belongs to the depositor. And when the depositor is the owner of the deposit property, the depositee's confirmation of the buyer's statement is effectively an acknowledgment of the property of another (the depositor) for the buyer, and acknowledgment of another's property for someone else is not valid. Therefore, the statements of the depositee and the buyer will not be believed against the depositor, and consequently, the depositee will not be ordered to give the deposit to the buyer.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage addresses a claim of purchase regarding a deposit while the depositor is still alive. The scenario: a person claims to have bought the deposited property from the depositor. The depositee confirms this claim. The ruling is that the depositee is not ordered to hand over the deposit to the buyer. The reason is that the depositor is alive and remains the owner of the deposit. The depositee's confirmation would be an acknowledgment of the buyer's right to the property that belongs to another (the depositor). Such an acknowledgment is invalid because it affects a third party's rights without their consent. Therefore, the depositee and the buyer cannot collude to transfer ownership. The passage reinforces the principle that a trustee's confirmation of a claim that contradicts the owner's rights is not effective. The ruling contrasts with the inheritance case where the owner is dead, so the ownership has ended.

Key Terms
شراء (shirā’): Purchase; the act of buying.
مدعی شراء (mudda‘ī shirā’): The claimant of purchase; the person who alleges he bought the deposit.
ملک (milk): Ownership; the legal right of possession and disposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the buyer did not provide witnesses.
B) Because the depositor is alive and owns the deposit, so the depositee's confirmation cannot transfer ownership.
C) Because the depositee is not authorized to sell.
D) Because the claim is not believable.

Key Takeaway
The depositee's confirmation of a sale of the deposit is ineffective while the depositor lives because the depositor remains the owner and the confirmation is an invalid acknowledgment of another's property.

Debt Collection Agent: Debtor's Claim of Prior Payment
Ruling on debtor's claim that creditor already collected; agent must be paid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a debtor claims the creditor has already received payment, and explain why the agent must be paid immediately rather than delayed.

Core Idea
A debtor's claim of prior payment does not delay the agent's right to collect; the agent is paid immediately, and the debtor's recourse is against the principal via an oath.

This passage from the Hidayah presents a specific scenario in debt collection: a creditor appoints an agent to collect a debt, but the debtor claims the creditor already received payment. How should the law resolve the tension between the agent's established authority and the debtor's assertion? The Hanafi ruling prioritizes the agent's right to collect based on the established agency, while protecting the debtor by allowing him to pursue the principal for an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the claim
Recognize the three parties: creditor (rabb al-māl), agent (wakīl), and debtor (gharīm). The debtor claims that the creditor already received the debt (istīfā’).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The debtor must pay the agent immediately. The right of the agent to collect is not postponed despite the debtor's claim.
3
Identify the legal reasoning (dalil)
The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference). Agency is established (either by mutual agreement or by evidence), while the debtor's mere claim does not prove receipt. Therefore, the agent's right is not delayed.
The text mentions two ways agency can be proven: by mutual acknowledgment (tasāduq) or by evidence (bayyinah).
4
Trace the debtor's recourse
After paying the agent, the debtor may pursue the original creditor and administer an oath to him. This is to safeguard the debtor's interest. The agent, being a deputy, is not sworn.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools might require the agent to swear, but the Hanafi school holds the agent as a deputy, so no oath is needed from him.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; the Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فان وكل وكيلاً بقبض ماله فادعى الغريم ان صاحب المال قد استوفاه فانه يدفع المال اليه لان الوكالة قد يثبت بالتصادق والاستيفاء لم يثبت بمجرد دعواه فلا يؤخر الحق قال ويتبع رب المال فيستحلفه رعاية لجانبه ولا يستحلف الوكيل لانه نائبه ترجمہ اگر کسی نے اپنا مال وصول کرنے کیلئے کسی کو کیا کیا پھر قرضدار نے کہا کہ صاحب مال نے اپنا مال وصول کر لیا ہے تو مال ، وکیل کو دیدیا جائے گا اسلئے کہ وکالت ان دونوں کے اتفاق سے ثابت ہو گئی اور مال کو وصول کرنا محض اس کے دعوا بی سے ثابت نہیں ہوا لہذا حق کو موخر نہ کیا جائیگا۔ اور قرضدار ، قرض خواہ کے پیچھے پڑ کر اس سے قسم لے گا تاکہ قرضدار کی جانب کا لحاظ رہے اور وکیل سے قسم نہیں لی جائے گی اس لئے کہ وہ اپنے موکل کا نائب ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر قرض خواہ نے کسی آدمی کو اس بات پر وکیل کیا کہ قرضدار سے میرے قرضہ کا مال وصول کر لے پھر قرضدار نے دعوی کیا کہ قرض خواہ اپنا مال وصول کر چکا ہے تو اس صورت میں قرضدار اس کا مکلف ہوگا کہ مال قرض وکیل کو دے دے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ وکالت وکیل اور قرضدار دونوں کے اتفاق کر لینے سے ثابت ہو گئی ہے۔ بعض نسخوں میں بالتصادق کا لفظ نہیں ہے الہذا ان نسخوں کے مطابق بقول صاحب عنایہ یہ مطلب ہوگا کہ وکالت ، بینہ سے ثابت ہو گئی ہے اس لئے کہ مسئلہ اس صورت میں فرض کیا گیا ہے کہ وکیل نے اپنی وکالت کو بعینہ کے ذریعہ ثابت کر دیا ہو بہر حال وکیل کی وکالت ثابت ہوگئی اور قرض خواہ کا مال قرض وصول کر نا محض قرضدار کے دعوی سے قرض وصول کرنا ثابت نہیں ہوا تو وکیل کو مال قرض پر قبضہ کرنے کا جو حق تھا اس کو قرض خواہ سے قسم کھلانے تک مؤخر نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ بلکہ وکیل کو مال قرض دیگر اس حق کو فوراً ادا کیا جائے گا البتہ قرضدار قرض خواہ کا دامن گیر
He said: If he appointed an agent to collect his property, and then the debtor claimed that the owner of the property had already received it in full, then he (the debtor) shall pay the property to him (the agent), because agency may be established by mutual agreement, and the receipt in full is not established by his mere claim, so the right is not delayed. He said: And the owner of the property shall be pursued, and he (the debtor) shall administer an oath to him out of consideration for his side, and the agent shall not be sworn because he is his deputy. Translation: If someone appointed an agent to collect his property, and then the debtor said that the owner of the property had already received it, then the property shall be given to the agent, because agency is established by the agreement of both, and receiving the property is not established by the debtor's mere claim, so the right is not postponed. And the debtor will pursue the creditor and make him take an oath in consideration of the debtor's side, and the agent will not be sworn because he is the deputy of his principal. Explanation: The scenario is that if the creditor appointed a person as agent to collect the debt from the debtor, then the debtor claimed that the creditor had already received his money, then in this case the debtor will be obligated to give the debt money to the agent. The proof is that agency is established by the agreement of both the agent and the debtor. In some copies, the word 'bil-tasāduq' (by mutual agreement) is not present; according to those copies, by the statement of Sahib al-‘Inayah, it means that agency is established by evidence, because the case is assumed where the agent has proven his agency by testimony. In any case, the agency is established, and the creditor's receipt of the debt money is not established merely by the debtor's claim, so the agent's right to take possession of the debt money will not be postponed until the creditor takes an oath. Rather, the debt money will be given to the agent immediately. However, the debtor will pursue the creditor (for the oath).
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario in debt collection where the debtor claims that the creditor has already received payment, but the creditor has appointed an agent to collect. The ruling is that the debtor must still pay the agent. The reasoning: the agency is established (either by mutual consent or by evidence), and the debtor's mere claim of prior receipt is not sufficient to establish that the debt is settled. Therefore, the agent's right to collect is not delayed. The debtor then has recourse: he can pursue the original creditor and demand an oath from him regarding whether he indeed received the payment. The oath is administered to the creditor in consideration of the debtor's interest. The agent, being a deputy, is not required to swear an oath because he is not personally liable. This ruling ensures that the agent's authority is respected while protecting the debtor's right to challenge a false claim of receipt. The commentary notes a textual variance regarding the proof of agency, but concludes that whether by mutual agreement or by evidence, the agency is established and the payment must be made.

Key Terms
غريم (gharīm): Debtor; the person who owes the debt.
استيفاء (istīfā’): Receipt in full; the act of receiving what is due.
تصادق (tasāduq): Mutual agreement; both parties affirming the agency.
حلف (half): Oath; the act of swearing by God to affirm a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Demand a receipt from the agent.
B) Pursue the creditor and administer an oath to him.
C) File a case in court against the agent.
D) Nothing; the matter is closed.

Key Takeaway
A debtor's claim of prior payment does not suspend the agent's right to collect; the agent is paid immediately, and the debtor's remedy is against the principal via an oath.

Depositee's confirmation of sale invalid
Ruling on claim of purchase of deposited goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a depositee's confirmation of a claim of purchase and explain the reasoning based on the depositor's continuing ownership.

Core Idea
A depositee's acknowledgment of a sale does not transfer ownership; the depositor remains owner until death.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in Hanafi law regarding deposits. When a third party claims to have bought the deposit from the owner, the depositee's confirmation of that claim is legally ineffective. Why? Because the depositee is merely a trustee and cannot affect the depositor's ownership. Understanding this principle clarifies the limits of a depositee's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
There are three parties: the depositor (the original owner), the depositee (the custodian), and a claimant who asserts he bought the deposit from the depositor. The depositee confirms the claimant's statement.
2
Determine the depositor's ownership status
The depositor is alive and therefore retains full ownership. The depositee is merely a trustee (amin) with no power over ownership.
3
Analyze the depositee's confirmation
The depositee's confirmation is an acknowledgment that the deposit now belongs to the claimant. However, this is an acknowledgment of another's property (the depositor's) in favor of a third party.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule on acknowledgment
In Hanafi law, an acknowledgment of another's property without authorization is invalid and not binding against the owner. Therefore, the depositee's confirmation is void.
5
Conclude the ruling
The court will not order the depositee to hand over the deposit to the claimant. The claimant must prove his purchase directly against the depositor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور مدعی میراث اور مودع (بفتح الدال ) دونوں نے اس بات پر اتفاق کر لیا کہ یہ مال ودیعت وارث کا مال ہے پس جب دونوں نے اس پر اتفاق کر لیا تو وہ مال ، مدعی میراث کو دینا ضروری ہو گیا۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ اگر ایک شخص نے دعوی کیا کہ میں نے مودع ( بكسر الدال) سے مال ودیعت خرید لیا ہے اور مودَع ( فتح الدال) نے مدعی شراء کی تصدیق بھی کر دی تو مودع ( فتح الدال) کو یہ حکم نہ دیا جائے گا کہ مال ودیعت کو مدعی شراء کے سپرد کر دے کیونکہ مودع ( بكسر الدال ) جب تک زندہ ہے تب تک وہ مالک ہونے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے لہذا مال ودیعت کا مودع ( بكسر الدال ) ہی مالک ہے۔ اور جب مودع ( بكسر الدال ) مال ودیعت کا مالک ہے تو مودع ( بفتح الدال ) کا مدعی شراء کے قول کی تصدیق کرنا غیر یعنی مودع ( بكسر الدال ) کے مال کا مدعی شراء کیلئے اقرار کرنا ہوا اور غیر کے مال کا کسی دوسرے کیلئے اقرار کرنا چونکہ مودع ( بكسر الدال ) کے خلاف مودع ( بفتح الدال ) اور مدعی شراء کے قول کی تصدیق نہ کی جائے گی ۔ اور جب ان دونوں کے قول کی تصدیق نہیں کی گئی تو مودع ( بفتح الدال ) کو یہ حکم بھی نہ دیا جائیگا کہ وہ مدعی شراء کو مال ودیعت دیدے۔
And the claimant of inheritance and the depositee (with fatha on the dal) both agreed that this deposited property is the property of the heir. So when both agreed upon it, that property became necessary to be given to the claimant of inheritance. The author of Hidayah said: If a person claimed that he bought the deposited property from the depositor (with kasra on the dal) and the depositee (with fatha on the dal) also confirmed the claimant of purchase, then the depositee (with fatha on the dal) will not be ordered to hand over the deposited property to the claimant of purchase, because as long as the depositor (with kasra on the dal) is alive, he has the capacity to be the owner; therefore the depositor (with kasra on the dal) is the owner of the deposited property. And when the depositor (with kasra on the dal) is the owner, the depositee's (with fatha on the dal) confirmation of the claimant of purchase's statement is an acknowledgment of the property of another, i.e., of the depositor (with kasra on the dal) in favor of the claimant of purchase, and an acknowledgment of another's property in favor of another is not accepted against the depositor (with kasra on the dal). And when the statement of these two is not accepted, the depositee (with fatha on the dal) will also not be ordered to give the deposited property to the claimant of purchase.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding a deposit (wadi'ah) where a third party claims to have purchased it from the depositee. The passage first states a basic rule: if the claimant of inheritance and the depositee agree that the deposit belongs to the heir, the depositee must hand it over. Then it addresses a more complex case: someone claims to have bought the deposit from the depositor (the original owner), and the depositee confirms this claim. The ruling is that the depositee is not compelled to deliver the deposit to the buyer. The reasoning is that the depositor remains the owner as long as he is alive; the depositee is merely a trustee. Therefore, the depositee's confirmation is considered an invalid acknowledgment of another's property, which is not binding in Hanafi law. This protects the depositor's ownership rights. The practical outcome is that the buyer cannot force the depositee to hand over the goods based solely on the depositee's acknowledgment; the buyer would need to establish his claim against the depositor himself.

Key Terms
مودع (بكسر الدال) (mūdi‘ (with kasr al-dāl)): The depositor, the person who leaves a deposit (wadi'ah) with another for safekeeping.
مودع (بفتح الدال) (mūda‘ (with fatḥ al-dāl)): The depositee, the person who accepts custody of the deposit (wadi'ah).
ودیعت (wadī‘ah): Deposit; a contract where property is left with another for safekeeping without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a depositee's confirmation of a sale of the deposit is invalid because the depositee cannot transfer ownership of another's property.

Agent Returning Defective Slave: The Buyer's Oath Requirement
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether agent needs buyer's oath before returning defective slave when seller claims consent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on an agent returning a defective slave when the seller claims buyer consent, and differentiate the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When a seller claims buyer accepted a defect, the agent cannot return the item until the buyer swears—Abu Hanifa insists on caution due to irreversibility.

This passage addresses a nuance in agency law: what happens when the seller claims the buyer already consented to a defect, thus blocking the right of return? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two chief disciples on whether the buyer must take an oath before the agent can return the defective item. Understanding this dispute sharpens your appreciation of how judicial finality (qaḍā) and the possibility of rectification shape legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute's core question
Read the passage to determine what triggers the dispute: the seller claims the buyer consented to the defect. The agent wants to return the slave girl. Does the agent need the buyer's oath first?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and its basis
Abu Hanifa says the agent cannot return until the buyer swears he did not consent. Why? Because once the qadi orders the return, the judgment is irreversible (even if error appears later). So caution requires the oath beforehand.
The key is 'māḍin ʿalā al-ṣiḥḥa' – the judgment proceeds on validity.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and its basis
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani say the agent can return immediately, like in the debt case. Why? Because if error appears later, the judgment is void (bāṭil), so rectification is possible.
They analogize to the debt case where money can be recovered from the agent.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Abu Hanifa: return is delayed until buyer's oath. Under Sahibayn: return is immediate; the buyer later has a claim against the agent if the seller proves consent.
The agent's risk differs: under Sahibayn, the agent could be liable.
5
Note the additional nuance from Abu Yusuf
The text reports that according to Abu Yusuf, even in the debt case, the agent should delay until the oath is taken. This is a middle view emphasizing caution in both situations.
This shows the richness of intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Source Text

خریدی ہوئی باندی میں عیب کی وجہ سے رد کرنے کا کسی کو وکیل بنایا اور بائع نے دعوی کیا کہ مشتری اس عیب پر راضی ہو گیا تھا تو مشتری کی قسم کے بغیر وکیل معیب باندی کو بائع کی طرف واپس نہیں کر سکتا
قال ومن وكله بعيب في جارية فادعى البائع رضا المشترى لم يرد عليه حتى يحلف المشترى بخلاف مسألة الدين لان التدارك ممکن هنالک باسترداد ما قبضه الوكيل اذا ظهر الخطأ عند نكوله وفي الثانية غير ممكن لأن القضاً بالفسخ ماض على الصحة وان ظهر الخطأ عند ابي حنيفة كما هو مذهبه ولا يستحـلـف الـمـشـتـرى عنده بعد ذالك لانه لا يفيد واما عندهما قالوا يجب ان يتحد الجواب على هذا في الفصلين ولا يؤخر لان التدارك ممكن عندهما لبطلان القضأ وقيل الاصح عند ابي يوسف ان يؤخر في الفصلين لانه يعتبر النظر حتى يستخلف المشترى لو كان حاضراً من غير دعوى البائع فينتظر للنظر ترجمہ اگر کسی شخص کو باندی میں عیب کی وجہ سے (باندی) کو واپس کرنے کا وکیل کیا پھر بائع نے دعوی کیا مشتری کے راضی ہونے کا تو وکیل بائع پر واپس نہیں کر سکتا یہاں تک کہ مشتری سے قسم لی جائے۔ برخلاف مسئلہ قرض کے اس لئے کہ قرض کے مسئلہ میں تدارک ممکن ہے اس کو واپس لے کر جس پر وکیل نے قبضہ کیا ہے جبکہ قرض خواہ کے قسم سے انکار کے وقت خطأ ظاہر ہو گئی ہو اور دوسرے مسئلہ میں تدارک ممکن نہیں ہے کیونکہ ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک نسخ پر فیصلہ کرنا صحت پر جاری ہوگا۔ جیسا کہ ان کا مذہب ہے اور اس کے بعد امام صاحب کے نزدیک مشتری سے قسم نہیں لی جائے گی کیونکہ یہ مفید نہیں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک مشائخ نے کہا دونوں صورتوں میں حکم یکساں ہونا چاہئے۔ اور واپس کرنے کے حکم میں تاخیر نہ کی جائے۔ کیونکہ صاحبین کے نزدیک تدارک ممکن ہے اس لئے حکم قضا ، باطل ہے اور کہا گیا یہ ابو یوسف کے نزدیک دونوں صورتوں میں مؤخر کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ انتظار معتبر ہے یہاں تک کہ مشتری سے قسم لی جائے۔ بشرطیکہ بائع کے دعوی کے بغیر وہ حاضر ہو تو غور کرنے کے لئے انتظار کیا جائے گا۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی نے خریدی ہوئی باندی میں عیب پا کر ایک شخص کو بائع کی طرف باندی واپس کرنے کا وکیل کیا اور بائع نے دعوی کیا کہ مشتری اس عیب پر راضی ہو گیا تھا تو بحکم قضاء وکیل اس معیب باندی کو بائع کی طرف واپس نہیں کر سکتا یعنی قاضی اس باندی کو واپس کرنے کا حکم صادر نہیں کرے گا الا یہ کہ مشتری اس بات کی قسم کھائے کہ میں اس عیب پر راضی نہیں ہوا تھا اس کے
If someone appointed an agent to return a slave girl due to a defect, and the seller claimed that the buyer had consented to the defect, the agent cannot return the defective slave girl to the seller without the buyer's oath. He said: Whoever appointed him as an agent regarding a defect in a slave girl, and the seller claimed the buyer's consent, it is not returned to him until the buyer swears, contrary to the case of debt, because in that case, rectification is possible by taking back what the agent received if error becomes apparent upon the buyer's refusal, while in the second case it is not possible because the judgment of rescission is valid ex facie even if error becomes apparent according to Abu Hanifa, as is his madhhab, and after that, according to him, the buyer is not made to swear because it is useless. As for the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), they said: The answer must be the same in both chapters, and it should not be delayed because rectification is possible according to them due to the nullity of the judgment. And it is said: The correct view according to Abu Yusuf is that it should be delayed in both chapters because consideration is taken into account, until the buyer is made to swear if he were present without the seller's claim, so he waits for consideration. Translation: If someone appointed an agent to return a slave girl due to a defect, and the seller claimed that the buyer had consented, then the agent cannot return her to the seller until the buyer takes an oath. Unlike the case of debt, because in the debt case, rectification is possible by taking back what the agent received when error becomes apparent upon the creditor's refusal, but in this second case, rectification is not possible because according to Abu Hanifa, the judgment of rescission proceeds on validity even if error becomes apparent. As is his madhhab, and after that, according to Imam (Abu Hanifa), the buyer is not made to swear because it is useless. According to the two companions, the scholars said: The ruling should be the same in both cases, and there should be no delay because rectification is possible according to them due to the nullity of the judgment. And it is said: According to Abu Yusuf, it should be delayed in both cases because consideration is valid, until the buyer takes an oath, provided he is present without the seller's claim, then he waits for consideration. Explanation: The scenario is that if someone found a defect in a purchased slave girl and appointed a person as an agent to return her to the seller, and the seller claimed that the buyer had consented to the defect, then by qada (judicial order), the agent cannot return the defective slave girl to the seller, meaning the judge will not issue a ruling to return her unless the buyer takes an oath that he did not consent to the defect.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage addresses a ruling on agency for returning a defective slave girl and includes an intra-Hanafi dispute. The scenario: a buyer appoints an agent to return a slave girl to the seller due to a defect (khiyar al-'ayb). The seller then claims that the buyer had consented to the defect, which would void the right of return. The ruling: the agent cannot return the slave girl until the buyer swears that he did not consent. This differs from the earlier debt case, where the agent could collect immediately and the debtor later demands an oath from the creditor. The reason is the difference in reversibility: if the agent returns the slave girl and it later turns out the buyer had consented, the judicial rescission would be upheld as valid under Abu Hanifa's view, making it difficult to reverse. In the debt case, the money can easily be recovered from the agent if the creditor takes an oath. The passage then discusses the ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa holds that after the judgment of rescission is passed, it stands even if error appears, so the buyer is not later sworn (since it would be useless). The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the judgment is void if based on error, so rectification is possible; therefore, they say the ruling should be the same as in the debt case: no delay, and the agent returns immediately, with the buyer later able to claim from the agent if he can prove consent. There is also a view attributed to Abu Yusuf that in both cases, the execution should be delayed until the buyer takes an oath, to be cautious. The practical upshot: the judge will not order the return of the slave unless the buyer swears he did not consent, protecting the seller's rights.

Key Terms
عیب ('ayb): Defect in a purchased item that gives the buyer the option of return (khiyar al-'ayb).
رد (radd): Return; the act of returning a defective item to the seller to rescind the sale.
قضا (qaḍā): Judicial order or decree; here, the qadi's ruling on the rescission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why must the buyer take an oath before the agent returns the defective slave girl?
A) Because the buyer might be lying.
B) Because the judicial rescission is irreversible once ordered.
C) Because the seller has the right to demand an oath.
D) Because the agent cannot be trusted.

Key Takeaway
The central point is that when a seller claims buyer consent to a defect, the Hanafi school's precautionary rule (Abu Hanifa) requires the buyer's oath before the agent can return the item, to prevent irreversible legal consequences.
