Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Tahaluf in Iqala: Qiyas and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on mutual oaths when price is disputed after iqala, established by analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi qiyas extending tahaluf to iqala before repossession, and state the intra-Hanafi difference regarding repossession.

Core Idea
Tahaluf applies to iqala by qiyas before repossession; after repossession, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ from Muhammad.

The general rule of tahaluf in a sale dispute requires the commodity to be in existence and in buyer's hands. Iqala (mutual rescission) is a faskh (rescission) for the parties, not a new sale, so the hadith text does not directly apply. The Hanafi school extends the rule by qiyas when the seller has not repossessed. A further intra-Hanafi dispute arises: if the seller repossesses after iqala, does tahaluf still apply? This lesson unpacks the qiyas reasoning and the disagreement between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Consider: A buys a slave-girl, takes possession, then both parties mutually rescind (iqala). After iqala but before the seller takes possession, they dispute the price. The buyer claims the price was X, the seller claims Y. What is the ruling?
2
Recognize Why the Text Does Not Apply Directly
The hadith for tahaluf says: 'If the two parties differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take mutual oaths and return it.' This applies to an absolute sale. Iqala is a rescission (faskh) for the contracting parties, not a new sale. Therefore, the text does not directly cover iqala.
The nature of iqala as faskh is key to understanding the need for qiyas.
3
Trace the Qiyas
Even though the text does not apply directly, the Hanafis apply qiyas (analogy). The asl (original case) is the absolute sale with the commodity still in the buyer's possession. The far' (new case) is iqala before repossession. The 'illa (effective cause) is the existence of the commodity in the buyer's hands while the price is disputed. Both cases share this 'illa, so the same ruling applies: tahaluf and return of the original sale.
The qiyas is valid only before repossession; after repossession the 'illa changes.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Difference After Repossession
If the seller takes possession of the commodity after iqala, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that tahaluf no longer applies because the commodity is no longer in the buyer's hands. Imam Muhammad, however, holds that tahaluf still applies because he considers the text operative even after repossession, seeing the dispute itself as the operative cause.
This difference rests on the identification of the 'illa.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: no tahaluf after repossession. The original sale does not return, and the dispute is resolved by other means (e.g., plaintiff's proof).
Imam Muhammad's view is a valid minority opinion within the school.

ایک شخص نے باندی خریدی اور اس پر قبضہ بھی کر لیا پھر اقالہ کیا اور ثمن میں اختلاف ہو گیا دونوں قسم اٹھائیں گے اور پہلی بیع لوٹ آئے گی 
قال ومن اشترى جارية وقبضها ثم تقايلا ثم اختلفا فى الثمن فانهما يتحالفان ويعود البيع الأول ونحن ما اثبتنا التحالف فيه بالنص لانه ورد فى البيع المطلق والاقالة فسخ فى حق المتعاقدين وانما اثبتناه بالقياس لان المسئلة مفروضة قبل القبض والقياس يوافقه على مامر ولهذا نقيس الاجارة على البيع قبل القبض والوارث على العاقد والقيمة على العين فيما اذا استهلكه في يد البائع غير المشترى ولو قبض البائع المبيع بعد الاقالة فلا تحالف عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف خلافا لمحمد لانه يرى النص معلولا بعد القبض ايضا 
ترجمہ اگر ایک شخص نے ایک باندی خرید کر اس پر قبضہ کر لیا پھر دونوں نے مبیع کا اقالہ کیا پھر دونوں نے ثمن میں اختلاف کیا تو وہ دونوں قسم کھائیں گے اور بیع اول عود کرے گی اور اس اقالہ کی صورت میں ہم نے تحالف کو بذریعہ نص ثابت نہیں کیا ہے اس لئے کہ نص بیع مطلق میں وارد ہوئی ہے اور اقالہ عاقدین کے حق میں فسخ بیع ہے بلکہ ہم نے اقالہ میں تحالف کو قیاس سے ثابت کیا ہے کیونکہ مسئلہ قبضہ سے پہلے فرض کیا گیا ہے اور قیاس اس کے موافق ہے جیسا کہ گذر چکا اسی وجہ سے ہم اجارہ کو قبضہ سے پہلے بیع پر قیاس کرتے ہیں اور وارث کو عاقد پر اور اس صورت میں جبکہ مبیع کو مشتری کے علاوہ نے بائع کے قبضہ میں ہلاک کر دیا ہو تو قیمت کو مال عین پر قیاس کرتے ہیں اور اگر اقالہ کے بعد بائع نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا تو ابوحنیفہ اور ابو یوسف کے نزدیک تحالف نہیں ہوگا امام محمد کا اختلاف ہے کیونکہ امام محمد قبضہ کے بعد بھی نص کو معلول جانتے ہیں۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی شخص نے ایک باندی خریدی اور ثمن ادا کر کے باندی پر قبضہ کر لیا پھر بائع اور مشتری دونوں نے اقالہ کیا یعنی بیع کو فسخ کر دیا لیکن اقالہ کے بعد بائع نے مبیع پر قبضہ نہیں کیا بلکہ ثمن کی مقدار میں اختلاف ہو گیا چنانچہ مشتری نے کہا کہ ثمن ایک ہزار ہے لہذا تجھ پر ایک ہزار واپس کرنا لازم ہے تو ایسی صورت میں بائع اور مشتری دونوں سے قسم لی جائے گی اور تحالف کے بعد اقالہ ختم کر دیا جائے گا اور پہلی بیع عود کر آئے گی حتی کہ بائع کا حق اس ثمن سے متعلق ہو جائے گا جو ثمن مشتری نے بائع کو دیا تھا اور مشتری کا حق اس مبیع سے متعلق ہو جائے گا جو مبیع مشتری کے قبضہ میں ہے۔ سوال لیکن اس پر سوال ہو گا کہ نص یعنی اذا اختلف المتبايعان والسلعة قائمة تحالفا و ترادا تحالف کے سلسلہ میں وارد ہوئی ہے مگر یہ نص اقالہ کو شامل نہیں ہے بلکہ بیع مطلق کو شامل ہے اور جب ایسا ہے تو اقالہ میں تحالف کیونکر جاری ہوا۔ جواب اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ہم نے اقالہ کے اندر تحالف نص سے ثابت نہیں کیا کیونکہ نص تو بیع مطلق یعنی بیع من كل وجہ کے بارے میں وارد ہوئی ہے اور اقالہ من كل وجہ بیع نہیں ہے کیونکہ اقالہ غیر عاقدین کے حق میں اگر چہ بیع جدید ہے لیکن عاقدین کے حق میں فسخ بیع ہے۔ بہر حال اقالہ میں ہم نے نص کے ذریعہ تحالف ثابت کیا ہے کیونکہ مسئلہ یہ فرض کیا گیا ہے کہ اقالہ کے بعد
A person bought a slave-girl and took possession of her, then they made an iqala (mutual rescission), then they differed regarding the price; they both will take oaths and the first sale will return.
He said: And whoever buys a slave-girl and takes possession of her, then they mutually rescind, then differ regarding the price, they take mutual oaths and the original sale returns. We did not establish tahaluf in this by text, because the text came regarding absolute sale, and iqala is a rescission (faskh) in the view of the contracting parties. Rather we established it by analogy, because the issue is assumed before possession, and analogy supports it as previously mentioned. For this reason we analogize ijar (hire) to sale before possession, and the heir to the contracting party, and value (qima) to the physical item (ayn) in the case where [the item] is destroyed in the seller's possession by someone other than the buyer. And if the seller took possession of the sold item after the iqala, there is no tahaluf according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, because he considers the text to be operative (ma'lul) even after possession.
Translation: If a person bought a slave-girl and took possession of her after paying the price, then both the seller and buyer made iqala, i.e., rescinded the sale, but after the iqala the seller did not take possession of the slave-girl, and they differed regarding the amount of the price. For example, the buyer said: The price was one thousand, so you must return one thousand to me. In this case, both the seller and buyer will take oaths, and after tahaluf the iqala will be terminated, and the first sale will return, so that the seller's right attaches to the price that the buyer gave him, and the buyer's right attaches to the slave-girl that is in his possession. Question: But it will be objected that the text, i.e., 'If the two contracting parties differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take mutual oaths and return it' was revealed regarding tahaluf, but this text does not include iqala; rather it includes absolute sale. When this is the case, how is tahaluf applied in iqala? Answer: The answer is that we did not establish tahaluf in iqala by text, because the text is regarding absolute sale, i.e., sale in every respect, and iqala is not a sale in every respect, because iqala, although it is a new sale for non-contracting parties, is a rescission of the sale for the contracting parties. In any case, we have established tahaluf in iqala through analogy, because the issue is assumed after iqala but before repossession.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage handles the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding tahaluf (mutual oaths) after iqala (mutual rescission) of a sale. The core ruling: if iqala occurs and the seller has not yet repossessed the sold item, and then a price dispute arises, the parties take mutual oaths and the original sale is reinstated. This is derived by analogy (qiyas) to the absolute sale case where the item is still in the buyer's possession (as per the well-known hadith). The text for tahaluf explicitly mentions a standing commodity (sal'a qa'ima), which applies to absolute sales, not iqala, because iqala is a rescission, not a new sale for the parties. However, by qiyas, the same rule applies because before repossession, the situation resembles the original sale scenario. The passage then details a specific example: a slave-girl bought, possessed, then iqala without seller taking possession, price dispute arises — both swear, iqala voided, original sale returns. An objection is raised: why apply the text to iqala? The answer: it is by qiyas, not direct text. Then a crucial intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is mentioned: if after iqala the seller does take possession, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no tahaluf (because the item is now in seller's hand, text doesn't apply), while Imam Muhammad says tahaluf still applies because he considers the text operative even after repossession. This difference rests on whether the 'illa (effective cause) of the tahaluf rule remains after the seller's repossession.

Key Terms
اقالہ (iqala): Mutual rescission of a contract by both parties, considered a new sale for third parties but a rescission for the contracting parties.
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oath-taking by two disputing parties in a sale dispute, leading to rescission of the contract.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract, returning the parties to their pre-contract state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what is the ruling on tahaluf if the seller takes possession of the sold item after iqala and then a price dispute arises?
A) Tahaluf applies as usual
B) No tahaluf, the original sale does not return
C) Only the seller takes an oath
D) The buyer takes an oath alone

Key Takeaway
Tahaluf in iqala is established by qiyas, not direct text; after repossession, the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) rejects tahaluf.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
7 min
Tahaluf in Iqalah: Established by Qiyas, Not Nass
Explains tahaluf and qiyas in iqalah for regular sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why tahaluf after iqalah is established by qiyas and identify the common illa linking iqalah to absolute sale.

Core Idea
Tahaluf in iqalah is analogized to absolute sale because both involve price dispute before possession.

This lesson dives into a subtle but critical point in Hanafi sales law: how a mutual oath (tahaluf) applies after a contract rescission (iqalah). The ruling may seem to derive directly from the prophetic hadith, but the Hanafi school clarifies that iqalah is not an absolute sale—so the text cannot be applied literally. Instead, the ruling is extended by analogy (qiyas). We will walk through that analogy and see how it also governs leasing, inheritance, and compensation cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the issue
Read the opening sentence: after a sale, possession, and iqalah, if the parties dispute the price, they take an oath and the original sale returns. Note that this ruling is not derived from the explicit hadith text (nass) but from qiyas.
2
Recognize the limitation of the nass
The hadith 'When two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take an oath and return it' applies only to absolute sales. Iqalah is a rescission (faskh) for the contracting parties, so it is not a sale in every respect.
This is why direct textual application fails.
3
Trace the qiyas structure
Identify the original case (asl): absolute sale before possession. Identify the new case (far'): iqalah before the seller has taken back possession. The common illa is the dispute over price while the item is not in the seller's possession. Thus the ruling of tahaluf is transferred.
The Hanafi school uses the condition of 'before possession' as the effective cause.
4
Extend the analogy to other cases
The passage extends the same qiyas to ijarah (lease) before possession, to heirs disputing the price, and to cases where the sold item is destroyed by a third party and its value is disputed. In each, the lack of possession triggers the analogy.
These extensions show the principle's scope.

یہ نص اقالہ کو شامل نہیں ہے بلکہ بیع مطلق کو شامل ہے اور جب ایسا ہے تو اقالہ میں تحالف کیونکر جاری ہوا۔ جواب: اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ہم نے اقالہ کے اندر تخالف نص سے ثابت نہیں کیا کیونکہ نص ، تو بیع مطلق یعنی بیع من كل وجہ کے بارے میں وارد ہوئی ہے اور اقالہ من کل وجہ بیج نہیں ہے کیونکہ اقالہ غیر عاقدین کے حق میں اگر چہ بیچ جدید ہے لیکن عاقدین کے حق میں نسخ بیج ہے۔ بہر حال اقالہ میں ہم نے نص کے ذریعہ قیاس کے مطابق ہے تو اس پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اقالہ میں بھی قبضہ سے پہلے تحالف ثابت کر دیا گیا اسی وجہ سے ہم اجاره قبل القبض کو بیچ پر قیاس کرتے ہیں یعنی اگر موجر (اجارہ پر دینے والے) اور متاجر نے اجرت کی مقدار میں اختلاف کیا اور مستاجر نے معقود علیہ پر قبضہ نہیں کیا تو بیع قبل القبض پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اس میں بھی تحالف ثابت کر دیا گیا اور وارث کو عاقد پر قیاس کرتے ہیں۔ یعنی جب بائع اور مشتری کے وارثوں نے شمن میں اختلاف کیا اور مبیع پر مشتری کے وارث نے قبضہ نہیں کیا تو ان دونوں کے درمیان بھی قیاس کی وجہ سے تحالف جاری ہوگا۔ اور اسی طرح قیمت کو مال عین پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے یعنی جب بائع کے قبضہ میں مبیع کو مشتری کے علاوہ نے ہلاک کر دیا اور غیر مشتری اس کی قیمت کا ضامن ہو گیا تو یہ قیمت ، تلف کردہ مال بیچ کے قائم مقام ہو گی چنانچہ اگر مشتری کے اس قیمت پر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے بلاک کردہ بیع کے شمن کی مقدار میں اختلاف ہو گیا تو اس میں بھی تحالف جاری ہوگا اور اس کو تحالف پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے جو مبیع کی موجودگی میں جاری ہوتا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ اگرا قالہ کے بعد بائع نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا تو شیخین کے نزدیک تخالف جاری نہ ہوگا البتہ امام محمد کے نزدیک جاری ہوگا۔ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ نص یعنی از اختلف المتبايعان والسلعة قائمة تحالفا و ترادًا ـ انکار کے ساتھ معلول ہے، یعنی متحالف کی علت عاقدین میں سے ہر ایک کا دوسرے کے عقد کا انکار ہے اور یہ علت دونوں صورتوں میں موجود ہے خواہ صبیع پر مشتری نے قبضہ کیا ہو یا قبضہ نہ کیا ہو، پس جب علت دونوں صورتوں میں موجود ہے تو دونوں صورتوں میں تخالف جاری ہوگا۔
He said: Whoever buys a slave girl, takes possession, then they rescind (perform iqalah), then they dispute over the price, then they both take an oath and the first sale returns. We did not establish tahaluf in this by nass because the nass came for an absolute sale, and iqalah is a rescission (faskh) for the two contracting parties. We established it by qiyas because the case is assumed before possession, and qiyas agrees with what preceded. Hence we analogize ijarah to sale before possession, the heir to the contracting party, and the value to the specific good where the good is destroyed in the seller's hand by someone other than the buyer. If the seller takes possession of the sold item after iqalah, then there is no tahaluf according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, because he considers the nass to be effective after possession as well. Translation: If a person buys a slave girl and takes possession, then both perform iqalah, then they dispute over the price, then they both take an oath and the first sale returns. In this case of iqalah, we did not establish tahaluf by nass because the nass came for an absolute sale, and iqalah is an annulment of the sale for the contracting parties. Rather, we established tahaluf in iqalah by qiyas because the issue is assumed before possession, and qiyas agrees with it as has passed. For this reason, we analogize ijarah before possession to sale, the heir to the contracting party, and in the case where the sold item is destroyed in the seller's hand by someone other than the buyer, we analogize the value to the specific good. If the seller takes possession of the sold item after iqalah, then there is no tahaluf according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Imam Muhammad differs because he considers the nass to be effective after possession as well. Explanation: The issue is that if a person buys a slave girl and pays the price and takes possession, then the seller and buyer perform iqalah, but after iqalah the seller does not take possession of the sold item, and they dispute over the amount of the price: the buyer says the price was one thousand, so the seller must return one thousand. In this case, oaths are taken from both seller and buyer, and after tahaluf the iqalah is terminated and the first sale returns, so that the seller's right relates to the price that the buyer gave, and the buyer's right relates to the sold item that is in his possession. Question: But it may be objected that the nass (the hadith: 'When the two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take an oath and return it') came for tahaluf, but this nass does not include iqalah; it includes absolute sale. So how is tahaluf applied in iqalah? Answer: The answer is that we have not established tahaluf in iqalah by nass because the nass came about absolute sale (sale in every respect), and iqalah is not a sale in every respect because iqalah, for third parties, is a new sale, but for the contracting parties it is an annulment of the sale. In any case, we have established tahaluf in iqalah by nass? Actually, because the assumption is that after iqalah the seller has not taken possession of the sold item. It has passed in the first chapter that tahaluf before possession is in accordance with qiyas. So when in absolute sale, before possession, tahaluf is in accordance with qiyas, then by analogizing to that, we establish tahaluf in iqalah before possession. For this reason, we analogize ijarah before possession to sale: if the lessor and lessee dispute over the amount of rent and the lessee has not taken possession of the subject matter, then analogizing to sale before possession, tahaluf is established. And we analogize the heir to the contracting party: when the heirs of the seller and buyer dispute over the price and the heir of the buyer has not taken possession of the sold item, then between them also tahaluf occurs by qiyas. Similarly, we analogize the value to the specific good: when the sold item is destroyed in the seller's hand by someone other than the buyer, and that third party becomes guarantor for its value, then this value stands in place of the destroyed good. So if a dispute arises over the amount of the price of the sale before the buyer takes possession of this value, then tahaluf also occurs in that, and it is analogized to tahaluf that occurs when the good is present. The author of Hidayah said: If after iqalah the seller takes possession of the sold item, then according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), tahaluf does not occur, but according to Imam Muhammad, it does occur. Imam Muhammad says: The nass (the hadith) is effective with the cause: the cause of tahaluf is the denial of each party to the contract, and this cause exists in both cases, whether the buyer has taken possession of the sold item or not. So when the cause exists in both cases, tahaluf occurs in both cases.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah is a ruling with proof, dealing with the application of mutual oath (tahaluf) after an iqalah (rescission of sale) when the parties dispute the price. The author first states the ruling: if a person buys a slave girl, takes possession, then they rescind the sale (iqalah), and then they dispute over the amount of the price (thaman), both parties must take an oath (yatalufan) and the original sale is reinstated. This is a critical point: the iqalah is nullified and the original transaction returns. The author then explains that this ruling is not based on the explicit text (nass) of the hadith about tahaluf in absolute sales, because iqalah is not a sale in every respect—it is a rescission (faskh) for the contracting parties, though it is considered a new sale for third parties. Instead, the Hanafi school establishes tahaluf here by analogy (qiyas), analogizing iqalah before the seller has taken back possession to an absolute sale before possession, where tahaluf is also established by qiyas. The author then extends this analogy to other cases: ijarah (lease) before possession, heirs of the contracting parties, and when the sold item is destroyed by a third party and its value is at issue. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the seller takes possession of the sold item after iqalah, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that no tahaluf occurs, while Imam Muhammad argues that it does occur, because he considers the cause of tahaluf—mutual denial—to exist in both cases. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as the later text indicates. Students must understand that iqalah is a distinct transaction that is treated like a sale in some respects but not others, and the condition of possession before tahaluf is key. The author uses the illustrative example of a slave girl, a common commodity in classical Hanafi texts, and the commentary clarifies that the original sale returns only when the seller has not yet reclaimed possession.

Key Terms
اقالة (iqalah): A mutual rescission of a sale contract by both parties. In Hanafi law, it is considered a faskh (annulment) for the contracting parties but a new sale for third parties.
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oath-taking by both parties when they dispute the price (thaman) after a sale (or iqalah) and the commodity is still in existence. Each party swears that their claim is true, and then the contract is rescinded.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') based on a common effective cause (illa). Here, iqalah before possession is analogized to absolute sale before possession.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of a contract. In the context of iqalah, it means the original sale is terminated and the parties return to their pre-sale positions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the basis for establishing tahaluf after iqalah when the seller has not taken possession?
A) Direct application of the hadith 'When two parties differ and the commodity is present...'
B) Consensus (ijma) of the Companions
C) Qiyas (analogy) to absolute sale before possession
D) Istihsan (juristic preference)

Key Takeaway
Tahaluf after iqalah before possession is wajib by qiyas, analogizing the dispute to an absolute sale before possession; this qiyas also extends to ijarah, heirs, and value disputes.

Dispute on Tahaluf After Possession in Iqalah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on condition of possession after iqalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad on whether tahaluf applies after the seller takes possession post-iqalah, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Possession after iqalah negates tahaluf for the Shaykhayn, but Imam Muhammad sees the cause of denial as continuous.

Once iqalah is complete and the seller reclaims the sold item, a dispute may still arise over the price. This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: the two senior imams (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that no mutual oath is required after possession, while Imam Muhammad insists that it is. Understanding this difference sharpens your ability to apply the school's relied-upon position in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the triggering condition
The source text notes a key condition: 'if the seller takes possession of the sold item after iqalah'. This changes the legal landscape.
2
State the Shaykhayn's position
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: No tahaluf. They hold that the analogy (qiyas) to absolute sale before possession breaks once possession shifts to the seller.
The nass is restricted to the pre-possession scenario.
3
State Imam Muhammad's position
Imam Muhammad: Tahaluf does occur. He considers the underlying cause (illa) of tahaluf to be the mutual denial (inkar) of each party, which exists regardless of who holds the item.
He sees the nass as operative after possession as well.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if possession is taken, then according to the Shaykhayn, the dispute is resolved by the seller's claim (since he has the item), but according to Muhammad, oaths are still required and the original sale may be reinstated if both swear.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (no tahaluf after possession). Later authorities like the author of al-Hidayah consider this the stronger view.

یعنی بیع من كل وجہ کے بارے میں وارد ہوئی ہے اور اقالہ من کل وجہ بیج نہیں ہے کیونکہ اقالہ غیر عاقدین کے حق میں اگر چہ بیچ جدید ہے لیکن عاقدین کے حق میں نسخ بیج ہے۔ بہر حال اقالہ میں ہم نے نص کے ذریعہ قیاس کے مطابق ہے تو اس پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اقالہ میں بھی قبضہ سے پہلے تحالف ثابت کر دیا گیا اسی وجہ سے ہم اجاره قبل القبض کو بیچ پر قیاس کرتے ہیں یعنی اگر موجر (اجارہ پر دینے والے) اور متاجر نے اجرت کی نے شمن میں اختلاف کیا اور مبیع پر مشتری کے وارث نے قبضہ نہیں کیا تو ان دونوں کے درمیان بھی قیاس کی وجہ سے تحالف جاری ہوگا۔ اور اسی طرح قیمت کو مال عین پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے یعنی جب بائع کے قبضہ میں مبیع کو مشتری کے علاوہ نے ہلاک کر دیا اور غیر مشتری اس کی قیمت کا ضامن ہو گیا تو یہ قیمت ، تلف کردہ مال بیچ کے قائم مقام ہو گی چنانچہ اگر مشتری کے اس قیمت پر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے بلاک کردہ بیع کے شمن کی مقدار میں اختلاف ہو گیا تو اس میں بھی تحالف جاری ہوگا اور اس کو تحالف پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے جو مبیع کی موجودگی میں جاری ہوتا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ اگرا قالہ کے بعد بائع نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا تو شیخین کے نزدیک تخالف جاری نہ ہوگا البتہ امام محمد کے نزدیک جاری ہوگا۔ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ نص یعنی از اختلف المتبايعان والسلعة قائمة تحالفا و ترادًا ـ انکار کے ساتھ معلول ہے، یعنی متحالف کی علت عاقدین میں سے ہر ایک کا دوسرے کے عقد کا انکار ہے اور یہ علت دونوں صورتوں میں موجود ہے خواہ صبیع پر مشتری نے قبضہ کیا ہو یا قبضہ نہ کیا ہو، پس جب علت دونوں صورتوں میں موجود ہے تو دونوں صورتوں میں تخالف جاری He said: Whoever buys a slave girl, takes possession, then they rescind (perform iqalah), then they dispute over the price, then they both take an oath and the first sale returns. We did not establish tahaluf in this by nass because the nass came for an absolute sale, and iqalah is a rescission (faskh) for the two contracting parties. We established it by qiyas because the case is assumed before possession, and qiyas agrees with what preceded. Hence we analogize ijarah to sale before possession, the heir to the contracting party, and the value to the specific good where the good is destroyed in the seller's hand by someone other than the buyer. If the seller takes possession of the sold item after iqalah, then there is no tahaluf according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, because he considers the nass to be effective after possession as well. Translation: If a person buys a slave girl and takes possession, then both perform iqalah, then they dispute over the price, then they both take an oath and the first sale returns. In this case of iqalah, we did not establish tahaluf by nass because the nass came for an absolute sale, and iqalah is an annulment of the sale for the contracting parties. Rather, we established tahaluf in iqalah by qiyas because the issue is assumed before possession, and qiyas agrees with it as has passed. For this reason, we analogize ijarah before possession to sale, the heir to the contracting party, and in the case where the sold item is destroyed in the seller's hand by someone other than the buyer, we analogize the value to the specific good. If the seller takes possession of the sold item after iqalah, then there is no tahaluf according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, but Imam Muhammad differs because he considers the nass to be effective after possession as well. Explanation: The issue is that if a person buys a slave girl and pays the price and takes possession, then the seller and buyer perform iqalah, but after iqalah the seller does not take possession of the sold item, and they dispute over the amount of the price: the buyer says the price was one thousand, so the seller must return one thousand. In this case, oaths are taken from both seller and buyer, and after tahaluf the iqalah is terminated and the first sale returns, so that the seller's right relates to the price that the buyer gave, and the buyer's right relates to the sold item that is in his possession. Question: But it may be objected that the nass (the hadith: 'When the two parties to a sale differ and the commodity is still in existence, they take an oath and return it') came for tahaluf, but this nass does not include iqalah; it includes absolute sale. So how is tahaluf applied in iqalah? Answer: The answer is that we have not established tahaluf in iqalah by nass because the nass came about absolute sale (sale in every respect), and iqalah is not a sale in every respect because iqalah, for third parties, is a new sale, but for the contracting parties it is an annulment of the sale. In any case, we have established tahaluf in iqalah by nass? Actually, because the assumption is that after iqalah the seller has not taken possession of the sold item. It has passed in the first chapter that tahaluf before possession is in accordance with qiyas. So when in absolute sale, before possession, tahaluf is in accordance with qiyas, then by analogizing to that, we establish tahaluf in iqalah before possession. For this reason, we analogize ijarah before possession to sale: if the lessor and lessee dispute over the amount of rent and the lessee has not taken possession of the subject matter, then analogizing to sale before possession, tahaluf is established. And we analogize the heir to the contracting party: when the heirs of the seller and buyer dispute over the price and the heir of the buyer has not taken possession of the sold item, then between them also tahaluf occurs by qiyas. Similarly, we analogize the value to the specific good: when the sold item is destroyed in the seller's hand by someone other than the buyer, and that third party becomes guarantor for its value, then this value stands in place of the destroyed good. So if a dispute arises over the amount of the price of the sale before the buyer takes possession of this value, then tahaluf also occurs in that, and it is analogized to tahaluf that occurs when the good is present. The author of Hidayah said: If after iqalah the seller takes possession of the sold item, then according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), tahaluf does not occur, but according to Imam Muhammad, it does occur. Imam Muhammad says: The nass (the hadith) is effective with the cause: the cause of tahaluf is the denial of each party to the contract, and this cause exists in both cases, whether the buyer has taken possession of the sold item or not. So when the cause exists in both cases, tahaluf occurs in both cases.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah is a ruling with proof, dealing with the application of mutual oath (tahaluf) after an iqalah (rescission of sale) when the parties dispute the price. The author first states the ruling: if a person buys a slave girl, takes possession, then they rescind the sale (iqalah), and then they dispute over the amount of the price (thaman), both parties must take an oath (yatalufan) and the original sale is reinstated. This is a critical point: the iqalah is nullified and the original transaction returns. The author then explains that this ruling is not based on the explicit text (nass) of the hadith about tahaluf in absolute sales, because iqalah is not a sale in every respect—it is a rescission (faskh) for the contracting parties, though it is considered a new sale for third parties. Instead, the Hanafi school establishes tahaluf here by analogy (qiyas), analogizing iqalah before the seller has taken back possession to an absolute sale before possession, where tahaluf is also established by qiyas. The author then extends this analogy to other cases: ijarah (lease) before possession, heirs of the contracting parties, and when the sold item is destroyed by a third party and its value is at issue. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: if the seller takes possession of the sold item after iqalah, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that no tahaluf occurs, while Imam Muhammad argues that it does occur, because he considers the cause of tahaluf—mutual denial—to exist in both cases. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as the later text indicates. Students must understand that iqalah is a distinct transaction that is treated like a sale in some respects but not others, and the condition of possession before tahaluf is key. The author uses the illustrative example of a slave girl, a common commodity in classical Hanafi texts, and the commentary clarifies that the original sale returns only when the seller has not yet reclaimed possession.

Key Terms
اقالة (iqalah): A mutual rescission of a sale contract by both parties. In Hanafi law, it is considered a faskh (annulment) for the contracting parties but a new sale for third parties.
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oath-taking by both parties when they dispute the price (thaman) after a sale (or iqalah) and the commodity is still in existence. Each party swears that their claim is true, and then the contract is rescinded.
شيخين (shaykhayn): The two senior imams: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, often contrasted with Imam Muhammad. Their opinion is generally given weight in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) ruling regarding tahaluf after the seller has taken possession of the sold item following an iqalah?
A) Tahaluf is wajib in all cases
B) No tahaluf is required
C) Tahaluf is optional
D) Imam Muhammad's view (tahaluf) is the mufta bihi

Key Takeaway
After the seller takes possession post-iqalah, the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that no tahaluf occurs, despite Imam Muhammad's contrary ijtihad.

The Effect of Iqalah in Salam
Ruling on iqalah in salam with qiyas proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on iqalah in salam and explain why salam does not return after iqalah, using the qiyas proof.

Core Idea
Iqalah in salam is an isqat (relinquishment), not a reversal; salam cannot be reinstated.

Salam is a special forward sale with unique rules. When the parties mutually rescind (iqalah) a salam contract, the rescission is considered a waiver of the right to demand delivery, not a cancellation that restores the original state. This lesson clarifies that after such iqalah, the salam contract does not return, even if a dispute arises over the price. The author provides a clear qiyas to the case of returning defective capital to highlight the difference from regular sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on iqalah in salam
The passage states that if parties perform iqalah on a salam contract and then dispute the price, the word of the muslam ilayh (seller) is accepted, and the salam contract does not return (is not reinstated). Determine the operative ruling: the salam is terminated permanently.
This ruling applies specifically to salam, not to regular sales.
2
Understand the nature of iqalah in salam
The author explains the underlying reason: iqalah in salam is an isqat (relinquishment) of the right to receive the goods. Unlike iqalah in a regular sale, which can be annulled (naqd), iqalah in salam does not admit of annulment. Recognize that this makes the contract termination final.
Isqat is a key Hanafi concept meaning a bilateral waiver that is irrevocable.
3
Examine the qiyas (analogical proof)
The author uses a precedent: if the capital of salam were a commodity and it was returned due to a defect and then perished before delivery, the salam does not return. This shows that in salam, the contract does not revert even if the capital is returned. Similarly, iqalah is like a return, so the salam does not return. Trace the analogy: the illah (effective cause) is the nature of salam as a forward sale where the subject matter is a debt/liability, not a specific object.
The qiyas is from the case of defect (al-ayb) to iqalah, showing consistency in the rule.
4
Contrast with regular sale
The author contrasts this with iqalah in a regular sale (bay' 'ayn). In a regular sale, if the subject matter (a specific item) is returned due to defect and then perishes, the sale is reinstated. This counterexample reinforces the special status of salam. Understand the practical difference: in salam, the subject matter is not a specific existing item but a future deliverable, so return does not revive the contract.
This contrast is crucial for application: never conflate salam with ordinary sales.

Source Text

قال ومن اسلم عشرة دراهم فى كر حنطة ثم تقايلا ثم اختلفا فى الثمن فالقول قول المسلم اليه ولا يعود السلم لان الاقالة فى باب السلم لا تحتمل النقض لانه اسقاط فلا يعود السلم بخلاف الاقالة في البيع الا يرى ان رأس مال السلم لو كان عرضا فرده بالعيب و هلک قبل التسليم الى رب السلم لا يعود السلم ولوكان ذالك فى بيع العين يعود البيع دل على الفرق بـ ترجمہ: اگر ایک شخص نے ایک کر گندم میں دس درہم بیع سلم کے طور پر دیئے پھر دونوں نے اقالہ کیا پھر شمن میں اختلاف کیا تو مسلم الیہ کا قول قبول ہو گا اور بیع سلم عود نہیں کرے گی اس لئے کہ باب سلم میں اقال نقض کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا ہے کیونکہ وہ اسقاط کے قبیل سے ہے پس سلم عود نہیں کرے گی برخلاف بیج میں اقالہ کے کیا نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ اگر مسلم کا راس المال کوئی سامان ہو پھر اس کو عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کر دیا اور رب السلم کو دینے سے پہلے مسلم الیہ کے قبضہ میں ہلاک ہو گیا تو سلم عود نہیں کرتی ہے اور اگر یہ معاملہ بیچ معین میں ہو تو بیع عود کر آتی ۔
He said: Whoever gives ten dirhams as salam for a kurr of wheat, then they rescind (iqalah), then they dispute over the price, then the word is the word of the muslam ilayh (the seller in salam), and the salam does not return (i.e., is not reinstated). Because iqalah in the chapter of salam does not admit of annulment (naqd), for it is an isqat (relinquishment). So the salam does not return, contrary to iqalah in a sale. Do you not see that if the capital of salam (ra's al-mal) was a commodity and it was returned due to a defect, and it was destroyed before delivery to the owner of the salam (rabb al-salam), the salam does not return. If that were in a specific sale, the sale would return. This indicates the difference. Translation: If a person gave ten dirhams as salam for a kurr of wheat, then both rescinded (iqalah), then they disputed over the price, then the word of the muslam ilayh is accepted, and the salam sale does not return. Because iqalah in the chapter of salam does not admit of annulment, for it is of the category of isqat (relinquishment). So the salam does not return, contrary to iqalah in a sale. Do you not see that if the capital of salam was a commodity and it was returned due to a defect, and it was destroyed before delivery to the owner of the salam, the salam does not return. If that were in a specific sale, the sale would return.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents a ruling with proof regarding iqalah in a salam (forward sale) contract. The author states that if a person gives ten dirhams as capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract for a kurr of wheat, then both parties perform iqalah (mutual rescission), and then they dispute over the price (thaman), the word of the muslam ilayh (the seller) is accepted, and the salam contract does not return. The reasoning is that iqalah in salam is considered an isqat (relinquishment) of the right to receive the delivered goods, not a rescission that can be reversed. Therefore, if the parties later dispute the price, the original salam is not reinstated; instead, the muslam ilayh's statement is taken as true (since he is the one who would have to return the capital). This contrasts with iqalah in a regular sale (bay' 'ayn), where the sale can return if there is a dispute, as discussed in the previous lesson. The author illustrates the difference with an example: if the capital of salam was a commodity and it was returned due to a defect, and then it perishes before delivery, the salam contract does not return (i.e., the risk is on the muslam ilayh). In a specific sale, if a similar situation occurred, the sale would return. This highlights the unique nature of salam contracts in Hanafi fiqh: salam is a form of sale where the subject matter is a future delivery, and iqalah in salam operates as a waiver of the right to demand delivery, rather than a cancellation that restores the status quo. Students should note that this ruling is specific to salam and does not apply to other sales.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price (ra's al-mal) is paid in advance for specified goods to be delivered at a future date. It is a form of sale with immediate payment and deferred delivery.
مسلم اليه (muslam ilayh): The seller in a salam contract, who receives the capital (ra's al-mal) and undertakes to deliver the specified goods at the future date.
اسقاط (isqat): Relinquishment or waiver of a right. In salam iqalah, it means the parties waive their rights to demand delivery or refund, so the contract is terminated without possibility of reinstatement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, after iqalah, if the parties dispute the price, whose word is accepted and does the salam return?
A) The buyer's (rabb al-salam); the salam returns.
B) The seller's (muslam ilayh); the salam does not return.
C) Both take an oath; the salam returns by default.
D) The judge decides; the salam is void.

Key Takeaway
Iqalah in salam is an irrevocable isqat; the salam contract does not return, and the muslam ilayh's word is taken if a dispute arises.

Taḥāluf in Iqāla and Before Possession: Analogies from Sale
Explaining taḥāluf by qiyas in multiple contracts and the dispute after iqāla

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply qiyas to extend the ruling of taḥāluf from sale to iqāla, lease, heir disputes, and price disputes, and distinguish between the positions of the Shaykhayn and Imam Muhammad regarding taḥāluf after iqāla.

Core Idea
Taḥāluf applies by analogy before possession; after full rescission, the Shaykhayn rule it out.

This passage from al-Hidayah expands the application of taḥāluf (mutual oath-taking) by qiyas from a sale before possession to other transactions: iqāla, lease, disputes among heirs, and disputes over the price of a destroyed item. It then presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute about taḥāluf after iqāla when possession has been taken. Understanding these analogies is essential for correctly applying taḥāluf in a variety of contractual disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original ruling (asl)
Locate the base case: in a sale before possession, when parties dispute the price and have no evidence, taḥāluf applies. This is the established ruling from the first chapter of the Book of Claims.
The hadith 'When the two parties to a sale dispute while the goods are still in existence, they swear an oath and rescind the sale' is the textual basis.
2
Extract the illa (effective cause)
Determine why taḥāluf is applied in the original case. The illa is the mutual denial of each party's claim regarding the contract amount, coupled with the absence of proof.
Imam Muhammad extracts the illa as mere denial, while the Shaykhayn consider the ongoing contract status part of the illa.
3
Apply qiyas to new cases (furu)
Using the illa, analogize to: iqāla before possession, lease before possession, heirs disputing price before possession, and dispute over the value of a destroyed item standing in place of the item.
Each case shares the illa of mutual denial about the contractual sum before possession.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on iqāla after possession
Imam Muhammad holds that taḥāluf still applies because denial exists. The Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) disagree, arguing that after iqāla and possession, the contract is fully rescinded, so the dispute is not over an ongoing contract.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that of the Shaykhayn.
5
Determine the practical effect (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the Shaykhayn, if parties dispute after iqāla and possession, no taḥāluf; case is decided by evidence. Under Imam Muhammad, taḥāluf is performed and the contract would be rescinded again.
This difference affects how judges handle such disputes.

Source Text

كتاب الدعوى

بائع نے مبیع پر قبضہ نہیں کیا ہے اول باب میں گذر چکا ہے کہ تخالف قبضہ سے پہلے قیاس کے مطابق ہے پس جب بیع مطلق میں قبضہ سے پہلے تحالف قیاس کے مطابق ہے تو اس پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اقالہ میں بھی قبضہ سے پہلے تحالف ثابت کر دیا گیا اسی وجہ سے ہم اجاره قبل القبض کو بیچ پر قیاس کرتے ہیں یعنی اگر موجر (اجارہ پر دینے والے) اور متاجر نے اجرت کی مقدار میں اختلاف کیا اور مستاجر نے معقود علیہ پر قبضہ نہیں کیا تو بیع قبل القبض پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے اس میں بھی تحالف ثابت کر دیا گیا اور وارث کو عاقد پر قیاس کرتے ہیں۔
یعنی جب بائع اور مشتری کے وارثوں نے شمن میں اختلاف کیا اور مبیع پر مشتری کے وارث نے قبضہ نہیں کیا تو ان دونوں کے درمیان بھی قیاس کی وجہ سے تحالف جاری ہوگا۔
اور اسی طرح قیمت کو مال عین پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے یعنی جب بائع کے قبضہ میں مبیع کو مشتری کے علاوہ نے ہلاک کر دیا اور غیر مشتری اس کی قیمت کا ضامن ہو گیا تو یہ قیمت ، تلف کردہ مال بیچ کے قائم مقام ہو گی چنانچہ اگر مشتری کے اس قیمت پر قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے بلاک کردہ بیع کے شمن کی مقدار میں اختلاف ہو گیا تو اس میں بھی تحالف جاری ہوگا اور اس کو تحالف پر قیاس کیا گیا ہے جو مبیع کی موجودگی میں جاری ہوتا ہے۔

صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا ہے کہ اگرا قالہ کے بعد بائع نے مبیع پر قبضہ کر لیا تو شیخین کے نزدیک تخالف جاری نہ ہوگا البتہ امام محمد کے نزدیک جاری ہوگا۔ امام محمد فرماتے ہیں کہ نص یعنی از اختلف المتبايعان والسلعة قائمة تحالفا و ترادًا ـ انکار کے ساتھ معلول ہے، یعنی متحالف کی علت عاقدین میں سے ہر ایک کا دوسرے کے عقد کا انکار ہے اور یہ علت دونوں صورتوں میں موجود ہے خواہ صبیع پر مشتری نے قبضہ کیا ہو یا قبضہ نہ کیا ہو، پس جب علت دونوں صورتوں میں موجود ہے تو دونوں صورتوں میں تخالف جاری ہوگا۔
Book of Claims. The seller has not taken possession of the sold item. It has passed in the first chapter that tahaluf before possession is according to analogy. So when in an absolute sale, tahaluf before possession is according to analogy, then by analogy to that, in iqala also, tahaluf before possession has been established. For this reason, we analogize lease before possession to sale: that is, if the lessor and lessee dispute the amount of rent and the lessee has not taken possession of the leased object, then by analogy to sale before possession, tahaluf is established in it as well. And we analogize the heir to the contracting party: that is, when the heirs of the seller and buyer dispute the price, and the buyer's heir has not taken possession, then between them also, due to analogy, tahaluf will apply. Similarly, the price is analogized to the specific property: that is, when the sold item is destroyed in the seller's possession by someone other than the buyer, and that other becomes liable for its value, then this value stands in place of the destroyed property. So if before the buyer takes possession of this value, a dispute arises about the amount of the price of the sold item, then in that case also tahaluf will apply, and it is analogized to the tahaluf that applies when the sold item exists. The author of al-Hidayah has stated: If after iqala, the seller takes possession of the sold item, then according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), tahaluf will not apply, but according to Imam Muhammad, it will apply. Imam Muhammad says that the text 'When the two parties to a sale dispute while the goods are still in existence, they swear an oath and rescind the sale' is made conditional on denial, meaning the cause for tahaluf is each party's denial of the other's contract, and this cause exists in both cases, whether the buyer has taken possession or not. So when the cause exists in both cases, tahaluf applies in both cases.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the book of claims (da'wa) from al-Hidayah, expanding on the application of tahaluf (mutual oath-taking) in various transactions by way of qiyas (analogy). The author begins by establishing the general principle: in a standard sale (bay' mutlaq), when the parties dispute before the buyer takes possession, tahaluf is applied according to qiyas. This is the original ruling from the first chapter of the book of claims. The author then extends this ruling by qiyas to iqala (rescission of a contract) before possession. The basis is that iqala is similar to sale in many respects, so the same rules apply. Next, the analogy is extended to lease (ijara): if the lessor and lessee dispute the rent amount before the lessee takes possession, tahaluf applies by analogy to sale before possession. Then, the heir is analogized to the contracting party: if the heirs of the seller and buyer dispute the price before the buyer's heir has taken possession, tahaluf applies. Finally, the price is analogized to specific property: if a non-buyer destroys the sold item and becomes liable for its value, this value is treated like the sold item itself; so if a dispute arises about the price before the buyer takes possession of the value, tahaluf applies. The passage then presents a key intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the application of tahaluf after iqala when the seller has taken possession. According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), tahaluf does not apply because the contract is fully rescinded and possession has been transferred. According to Imam Muhammad, tahaluf still applies because the cause (illa) for tahaluf is the denial of the contract, and that denial exists regardless of possession. Imam Muhammad supports his view by citing the hadith principle: 'When the two parties to a sale dispute while the goods are still in existence, they swear an oath and rescind the sale'—he argues that the illa is the mutual denial, which is present in both situations. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of the Shaykhayn, as it is more rigorously based on the principle that after full rescission and delivery, the dispute is over a concluded matter, not an ongoing contract.

Key Terms
تحالف (taḥāluf): A procedure where both parties to a dispute take oaths, leading to rescission of the contract if they disagree.
إقالة (iqāla): Mutual rescission of a contract by agreement of the parties.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a common effective cause ('illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), does taḥāluf apply after iqāla if the seller has taken possession of the sold item?
A) Yes, taḥāluf applies based on analogy.
B) No, taḥāluf does not apply because the contract has been fully rescinded.
C) Only if the buyer demands it.
D) Taḥāluf applies only if both parties agree.

Key Takeaway
Taḥāluf is extended by analogy to various contracts before possession, but after full rescission and delivery, the ruling changes according to the Shaykhayn (mufta bihi).

8 min
Iqāla in Salam: An Irreversible Dropping of Debt
Ruling on dispute after iqāla in salam: no restoration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of iqāla in a salam contract, explain why it differs from iqāla in a regular sale, and apply the ruling to a dispute scenario.

Core Idea
Iqāla in salam is isqāt (dropping) of the debt, irreversible; the contract never returns.

When parties to a salam contract mutually rescind (iqāla) and then dispute the price, a key question arises: does the contract revive? The Hanafi school rules that it does not, because iqāla in salam is a pure dropping of a debt (isqāt), not a reversible dissolution. This lesson unpacks that ruling, its evidence, and the critical difference from iqāla in regular sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the act is iqāla (mutual rescission) in a salam contract. The ruling: after iqāla, if the parties dispute the amount of capital, the statement of the Muslim ilayh (seller) is accepted with his oath, and the salam contract is not restored.
The ruling is procedural and substantive: it determines whose word is taken and the effect on the contract.
2
Locate the dalil
The text uses an analogy: compare iqāla in salam to the case where the salam capital is a commodity that is returned due to a defect and destroyed before delivery. In that case, the salam is not restored. This shows that iqāla in salam is isqāt, not faskh.
The dalil is based on qiyas and a practical example, not a direct scriptural text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key point: in salam, the muslam fīh (delayed commodity) is a debt (dayn) in the liability of the Muslim ilayh. Iqāla drops this debt entirely. Since the debt no longer exists, there is no basis for the salam to revive. In contrast, a regular sale involves a specific object (ayn), so iqāla is a dissolution that can be reversed.
The nature of the subject matter (debt vs. specific object) determines the distinction.
4
Note the contrast with sale
Observe the explicit comparison: in a regular sale of a specific item, if iqāla occurs and the item is returned due to a defect, the sale is restored. This is because the item can be physically returned. The text concludes: 'This indicates the difference.'
The ruling is unique to salam; do not analogize salam iqāla to sale iqāla.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن اسلم عشرة دراهم فى كر حنطة ثم تقايلا ثم اختلفا فى الثمن فالقول قول المسلم اليه ولا يعود السلم لان الاقالة فى باب السلم لا تحتمل النقض لانه اسقاط فلا يعود السلم بخلاف الاقالة في البيع الا يرى ان رأس مال السلم لو كان عرضا فرده بالعيب و هلک قبل التسليم الى رب السلم لا يعود السلم ولوكان ذالك فى بيع العين يعود البيع دل على الفرق بـ

ترجمہ اگر ایک شخص نے ایک کر گندم میں دس درہم بیع سلم کے طور پر دیئے پھر دونوں نے اقالہ کیا پھر شمن میں اختلاف کیا تو مسلم الیہ کا قول قبول ہو گا اور بیع سلم عود نہیں کرے گی اس لئے کہ باب سلم میں اقال نقض کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا ہے کیونکہ وہ اسقاط کے قبیل سے ہے پس سلم عود نہیں کرے گی برخلاف بیج میں اقالہ کے کیا نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ اگر مسلم کا راس المال کوئی سامان ہو پھر اس کو عیب کی وجہ سے واپس کر دیا اور رب السلم کو دینے سے پہلے مسلم الیہ کے قبضہ میں ہلاک ہو گیا تو سلم عود نہیں کرتی ہے اور اگر یہ معاملہ بیچ معین میں ہو تو بیع عود کر آتی ۔

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک شخص نے ایک کر گندم میں دس درہم کے عوض عقد سلم کیا اور رب اسلم نے دس درہم مسلم الیہ کو دیدئیے پھر اختلاف کیا چنا نچہ مسلم الیہ نے کہا کہ رأس المال پانچ درہم تھے اور رب السلم نے کہا کہ دس درہم تھے تو اس صورت میں مسلم لیہ کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ رب السلم پانچ درہم زائد کا مدعی ہے اور مسلم الیہ اس کا منکر ہے اور بینہ موجود نہیں ہے لہذا منکر یعنی مسلم لیہ مع کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوگا اور نہ یہاں تحالف ہو گا اور نہ بیع سلم عود کرے گی کیونکہ باب سلم میں اقالہ نقض اور ٹوٹنے کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا ہے اس لئے کہ اقالہ اس مسلم فیہ کو ساقط کرنے کا نام ہے جو ابھی مسلم الیہ کے ذمہ میں دین ہے پس جب اقالہ کے ذریعہ مسلم فیہ ساقط کر دی گئی تو مسلم کیسے عود کر سکتی ہے کیونکہ سلم کے لئے مسلم الیہ کے ذمہ مسلم فیہ کا ہونا ضروری ہے اس کے برخلاف بیع میں اقالہ کے کیونکہ بیع میں مبیع عین ہوتی ہے لہذا ئج کا اقالہ مسخ ہو کر مبیع مشتری کی طرف واپس آسکتی ہے اور جب میج مشتری کی طرف واپس آئے گی تو بیع عود کر آئی اس کی وضاحت کرتے ہوئے فرمایا کہ اگر سلم کا راس المال سامان ہو اور پھر عیب کیوجہ سے قاضی نے اس کو رب السلام کی طرف واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ کر دیا ہومگر رب السلام کو دینے سے پہلے وہ سامان مسلم الیہ کے قبضہ میں ہلاک ہو گیا تو سلم عود نہیں کرے گی اور یہ معاملہ مبیع مطلق میں ہوتا ہے تو بیع عود کر آتی ہے پس سلم اور بیع کے درمیان یہی فرق ہے۔
He said: And whoever pays ten dirhams as salam for one kurr of wheat, then they mutually rescind (iqāla), then they dispute about the price, then the statement is that of the Muslim ilayh (the party receiving the salam capital), and the salam is not restored. Because iqāla in the chapter of salam does not admit of annulment, as it is (an) isqāt (dropping of a right), so the salam is not restored, unlike iqāla in sale. Do you not see that if the capital of salam were a commodity, then he returns it due to a defect, and it is destroyed before delivery to the rabb al-salam (the party paying the capital), the salam is not restored. And if that were in a sale of a specific item, the sale would be restored. That indicates the difference.

Translation: If a person paid ten dirhams as salam for one kurr of wheat, then both rescinded, then disputed about the price, then the statement of the Muslim ilayh is accepted, and the salam contract is not restored. Because in the chapter of salam, iqāla does not admit of annulment, as it is of the category of isqāt. So the salam is not restored, contrary to iqāla in sale. Do you not see that if the salam capital is a commodity, and then he returns it due to a defect, and before delivering it to the rabb al-salam it is destroyed in the possession of the Muslim ilayh, then the salam is not restored. And if this were in a sale of a specific item, the sale would be restored.

Explanation of the case: It is that if a person made a salam contract for one kurr of wheat for ten dirhams, and the rabb al-salam gave the ten dirhams to the Muslim ilayh, then they disputed: the Muslim ilayh said the capital was five dirhams, and the rabb al-salam said it was ten dirhams. In this case, the statement of the Muslim ilayh with his oath is accepted, because the rabb al-salam is claiming five extra dirhams, and the Muslim ilayh denies it. When there is no evidence, the denial of the Muslim ilayh with oath is accepted. Neither is there tahaluf here, nor is the salam restored, because in the chapter of salam, iqāla does not admit of annulment and dissolution, because iqāla is the name of dropping the muslam fīh which is a debt in the liability of the Muslim ilayh. So when the muslam fīh has been dropped through iqāla, how can the salam be restored? Because for salam, it is necessary that the muslam fīh be in the liability of the Muslim ilayh. Contrary to iqāla in sale, because in sale, the sold item is a specific object, so the iqāla of sale can be overturned and the sold item can return to the buyer. When the sold item returns to the buyer, the sale is restored. Clarifying this, he said: If the capital of salam is a commodity, and due to a defect the judge decides to return it to the rabb al-salam, but before delivering it to the rabb al-salam, that commodity is destroyed in the possession of the Muslim ilayh, then the salam is not restored. And if this matter were in a sale of a specific item, the sale would be restored. Thus this is the difference between salam and sale.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage presents a specific ruling regarding a dispute after iqāla (mutual rescission) in a salam (advance payment sale) contract. The core issue is: after both parties rescind the salam contract, if they then dispute about the price (i.e., the capital), whose statement is accepted and does the salam contract revert? The ruling is clear: the statement of the Muslim ilayh (the seller who receives the capital and is obligated to deliver the commodity) is accepted with his oath, and the salam contract is not restored. The reasoning is that iqāla in salam is considered an isqāt (dropping) of the debt owed (the muslam fīh). Since it is a dropping of a right, it cannot be undone or revoked. Once the debt is dropped, there is no basis for the salam to be revived. This is contrasted with iqāla in a regular sale of a specific item (bay' al-'ayn). In such a sale, iqāla is a dissolution (faskh) that can be reversed: the sold item returns to the seller and the price returns to the buyer, effectively restoring the original sale. The passage then illustrates the principle with an example: if the salam capital was a commodity (not money), and the buyer returns it due to a defect, and before the seller returns it, it is destroyed in the seller's possession, then the salam is not restored—meaning the seller remains liable for the value, but the contract is not revived. In a regular sale, if a similar defect causes return, the sale is fully restored (the seller gets the item back and must return the price). The passage emphasizes that the nature of salam (a deferred delivery of a generic commodity against immediate payment) makes iqāla a pure dropping of the liability, which cannot be undone. Therefore, no tahaluf (mutual oaths) is required either, because the dispute is about the amount of capital after the contract has already been rescinded, and the priority is to the denial of the increased claim. The commentary further explains the procedural aspect: the Muslim ilayh denies the extra amount, so his statement with an oath is accepted; there is no need for mutual oaths because the contract is already terminated.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqāla): Mutual rescission of a contract; in salam, it is considered isqāt (dropping) of the debt, not a reversible dissolution.
سلم (salam): A forward contract where the price is paid in advance and the commodity is deferred; also called salaf.
إسقاط (isqāt): Dropping or relinquishment of a right; in this context, dropping the debt of the muslam fīh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After iqāla in a salam contract, if the parties dispute the amount of the capital (price), what happens to the salam contract?
A) The salam is restored and the parties must perform the contract.
B) The salam is not restored because iqāla in salam is an isqāt (dropping) of the debt.
C) The salam is restored only if the Muslim ilayh admits the claim.
D) The salam is automatically invalidated and no further action is needed.

Key Takeaway
Iqāla in salam is an irreversible dropping (isqāt) of the debt; therefore, the contract cannot be restored after mutual rescission.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Effect of Iqalah in Salam and Sale Contracts
Ruling on iqalah in salam vs sale with a dispute example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effect of iqalah in a salam contract from that in a sale contract and apply the rule of whose statement is accepted in a dispute over the capital amount.

Core Idea
Iqalah in salam cancels a debt and does not revert the contract; in sale, iqalah annuls and the sold item returns, reverting the contract.

In Hanafi fiqh, the nature of the subject matter in a contract determines the effect of iqalah (mutual rescission). This passage from the Book of Claims explains why iqalah in a salam contract is fundamentally different from iqalah in a regular sale. You will see how a dispute over the capital amount leads to a rule about whose statement is accepted, and how the concept of debt vs. specific property drives the legal outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed element
Examine the scenario: rabb al-salam claims capital was ten dirhams, muslam ilayh says five. The extra five dirhams are in dispute.
2
Apply the rule of denial
The muslam ilayh is the denier of the extra amount; his statement with oath is accepted because the claimant (rabb al-salam) lacks evidence.
No tahaluf (mutual oath) is needed here.
3
Analyze the effect of iqalah in salam
Iqalah cancels the muslam fihi (the goods to be delivered), which is a debt on the muslam ilayh. Since the debt is removed, the contract cannot revert.
4
Contrast with sale
In a regular sale, the subject is a specific tangible item. Iqalah annuls the sale, and the item returns to the seller, causing the contract to revert.

Source Text

پس یہ ان دونوں میں فرق کی دلیل ہے۔
تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک شخص نے ایک کر گندم میں دس درہم کے عوض عقد سلم کیا اور رب السلم نے دس درہم مسلم الیہ کو دے دیے پھر اختلاف کیا چنانچہ مسلم الیہ نے کہا کہ رأس المال پانچ درہم تھے اور رب السلم نے کہا کہ دس درہم تھے تو اس صورت میں مسلم الیہ کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ رب السلم پانچ درہم زائد کا مدعی ہے اور مسلم الیہ اس کا منکر ہے اور بینہ موجود نہیں ہے لہذا منکر یعنی مسلم الیہ کا قول مع الیمین معتبر ہوگا اور نہ یہاں تحالف ہو گا اور نہ سلم عود کرے گی کیونکہ باب سلم میں اقالہ نقض اور ٹوٹنے کا احتمال نہیں رکھتا ہے اس لئے کہ اقالہ اس مسلم فیہ کو ساقط کرنے کا نام ہے جو ابھی مسلم الیہ کے ذمہ میں دین ہے پس جب اقالہ کے ذریعہ مسلم فیہ ساقط کر دی گئی تو سلم کیسے عود کر سکتی ہے کیونکہ سلم کے لئے مسلم الیہ کے ذمہ مسلم فیہ کا ہونا ضروری ہے اس کے برخلاف بیع میں اقالہ کے کیونکہ بیع میں مبیع عین ہوتی ہے لہذا مبیع کا اقالہ مسخ ہو کر مبیع مشتری کی طرف واپس آ سکتی ہے اور جب مبیع مشتری کی طرف واپس آئے گی تو بیع عود کر آئی۔ اس کی وضاحت کرتے ہوئے فرمایا کہ اگر سلم کا راس المال سامان ہو اور پھر عیب کی وجہ سے قاضی نے اس کو رب السلم کی طرف واپس کرنے کا فیصلہ کر دیا ہو مگر رب السلم کو دینے سے پہلے وہ سامان مسلم الیہ کے قبضہ میں ہلاک ہو گیا تو سلم عود نہیں کرے گی اور یہ معاملہ مبیع مطلق میں ہوتا ہے تو بیع عود کر آتی ہے پس سلم اور بیع کے درمیان یہی فرق ہے۔
The book of claims. So this is the proof of the difference between the two. Explanation: The scenario is that if a person made a salam contract for one kurr of wheat in exchange for ten dirhams, and the rabb al-salam gave the ten dirhams to the muslam ilayh, then they disputed: the muslam ilayh said the capital was five dirhams and the rabb al-salam said it was ten dirhams. In this case, the word of the muslam ilayh with his oath is accepted because the rabb al-salam is claiming an extra five dirhams and the muslam ilayh denies it, and there is no evidence. Therefore, the denier, that is, the muslam ilayh, his word with his oath is accepted. There will be no mutual oath (tahāluf) here, nor will the salam contract revert, because in the chapter of salam, iqalah (rescission) does not entail annulment and breaking. This is because iqalah is the cancellation of the muslam fihi which is a debt on the muslam ilayh. So when the muslam fihi is cancelled by iqalah, how can the salam revert? Because for salam, the muslam fihi must be in the liability of the muslam ilayh. Unlike in sale, because in sale the sold item is a specific thing, so iqalah in sale annuls the sale and the sold item returns to the buyer, and when the sold item returns to the buyer, the sale reverts. Clarifying this, he said: If the capital of salam is goods, and then due to a defect the judge decides to return it to the rabb al-salam, but before giving it to the rabb al-salam, the goods perish in the possession of the muslam ilayh, then the salam does not revert. And in the case of an absolute sale, the sale does revert. So this is the difference between salam and sale.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with reasoning that distinguishes between salam (forward sale) and regular sale regarding the effect of iqalah (rescission). The author explains that in a salam contract, when there is a dispute over the capital (ra's al-mal), the word of the muslam ilayh (the seller who receives the capital) is taken with his oath, because he is the denier of the extra amount claimed by the rabb al-salam (the buyer). No mutual oath (tahāluf) is required, and the contract does not revert upon iqalah. The reason is that iqalah in salam cancels the muslam fihi (the goods to be delivered), which is a debt in the liability of the muslam ilayh. Since cancellation removes the subject matter, the contract cannot revert. In contrast, in a regular sale, the sold item is a specific tangible thing, so iqalah annuls the sale and the item returns to the buyer, causing the sale to revert. The passage also gives an example where the capital is goods: if the goods perish after a defect-based return, the salam does not revert, but a regular sale would. This is a key difference between the two contracts, rooted in the nature of the subject matter (debt vs. specific item).

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A forward sale where the price is paid immediately and the goods are delivered later; the goods are a debt on the seller.
رب السلم (rabb al-salam): The buyer who pays the capital (ra's al-mal) in a salam contract.
مسلم الیہ (muslam ilayh): The seller who receives the capital and is obligated to deliver the goods in a salam contract.
اقالہ (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract; cancellation by agreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a salam contract, if the rabb al-salam claims the capital was ten dirhams but the muslam ilayh says it was five, whose statement is accepted?
A) The rabb al-salam's statement without oath
B) The muslam ilayh's statement with oath
C) Both parties take oaths and the contract is rescinded
D) The statement of the person with evidence

Key Takeaway
Iqalah in salam cancels a debt and does not allow the contract to revert, unlike sale where iqalah nullifies the sale and returns the item.

Spousal Dispute Over Household Items: The Hanafi Allocation Rule
Ruling statement on spousal dispute resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for allocating household items in a spousal dispute without evidence, and apply the principles of qawl al-qabid and zahir al-hal.

Core Idea
In spousal disputes over household items, custom determines suitability, and the possessor's word is accepted.

When a husband and wife dispute over household items and neither has proof, the Hanafi school relies on apparent circumstances and possession. This lesson explains how items are allocated based on what is traditionally suitable for men, women, or both, and why the wife gets her specific items despite the husband's general possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of items
The passage classifies household items into three types: those befitting men, those befitting women, and those befitting both.
Examples given: turban, bow for men; dupatta, jewelry for women; vessels for both.
2
Apply the allocation rule per category
Men's items go to the husband after he takes an oath, women's items go to the wife after she takes an oath, and common items go to the husband.
The oath is required when there is no evidence.
3
Understand the reasoning for common items
Common items go to the husband because the wife and her possessions are under his general possession, and the word of the possessor (qawl al-qabid) is accepted in claims.
Zahir al-hal (apparent circumstance) also supports the husband as the primary possessor.
4
Analyze the exception for women's items
Women's items go to the wife despite the husband's general possession because her specific possession of these items is stronger than his general possession.
Analogized to two people disputing over a cloth: the one wearing it has stronger possession than the one holding the sleeve.
5
Confirm the temporal scope
The ruling applies whether the dispute occurs during marriage or after separation (e.g., divorce).
The author of Al-Hidayah explicitly states no difference in the ruling.

كتاب الدعوى
اشرف الہدایہ جلد دہم
کیونکہ ظاہر اسی کا شاہد ہے اور جو چیز میں عورتوں کے لائق ہیں وہ عورت کیلئے ہوں گی جیسے اوڑھنی کا سر بند ۔ کیونکہ ظاہر حال اس کا شاہد ہے۔ اور جو چیزیں دونوں کے لائق ہیں جیسے برتن تو وہ مرد کیلئے ہے۔ کیونکہ عورت اور جو کچھ اس کے قبضہ میں ہے سب مرد کے قبضہ میں ہے اور دعاوی میں قابض ہی کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ بر خلاف ان چیزوں کے جو عورتوں سے مختص ہیں اس لئے کہ اس کے معارض اس سے زیادہ قوی ظاہر ہے اور یہ اختلاف ان دونوں میں نکاح کے قائم ہونے کی حالت میں ہو یا فرقت واقع ہونے کے بعد ہو حکم میں کچھ فرق نہیں ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر میاں بیوی نے گھر کے سامان میں اختلاف کیا کہ یہ سامان میری ملک ہے اور بینہ دونوں کے پاس موجود نہیں ہے تو جو سامان مرد کے لائق ہے وہ سامان قسم لے کر مرد کو دیدیا جائے گا جیسے عمامہ ، ٹوپی ، کتابیں، قباء ، کمان ، آلات جہاد۔ کیونکہ اس طرح کے سامان میں ظاہری حال مرد ہی کا شاہد ہے اور دعاوی میں اسی کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے جس کا ظاہر حال شاہد ہوتا ہے۔ اور جو سامان عورتوں کے لائق ہے وہ سامان قسم لے کر عورت کو دیدیا جائے گا جیسی اوڑھنی اور اوڑھنی کا سر بند ، برقعہ اور زیور وغیرہ، کیونکہ اس میں عورت ہی کا شاہد ہے اور جو سامان دونوں کے لائق ہے وہ بھی مرد کو دیدیا جائے گا کیونکہ عورت اور مرد کے قبضہ کی چیزیں سب مرد کیلئے ہوتی ہیں۔ اور دعاوی میں قابض کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا مرد جو عورت اور عورت کی مقبوضہ چیزوں پر قابض ہے سب میں مرد کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ مگر جو سامان عورت کے ساتھ مختص ہوتا ہے وہ عورت کیلئے اس لئے ہو گا کہ ایک طرف تو شوہر کا ظاہری قبضہ ہے اور دوسری طرف عورت کا قبضہ ہے جو اس سے اقویٰ ہے، پس عورت کا قبضہ جو اقوئی ہے اسکوتر جیح حاصل ہوگی ۔ جیسے دو آدمیوں نے ایک کپڑے میں اختلاف کیا اور ایک اس کو پہنے ہوئے ہے اور دوسرا اس کی آستین پکڑے ہوئے ہے تو جو شخص پہنے ہوئے ہے اس کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ کہتے ہیں کہ یہ اختلاف نکاح کی موجودگی میں ہو یا فرقت واقع ہونے کے بعد ہو دونوں کے درمیان حکم میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔
Book of Claims. Because the apparent circumstance is a witness for him, and items befitting women are for the woman, such as the headband of a dupatta, because the apparent circumstance is a witness for her. And items befitting both, like a vessel, are for the man, because the woman and whatever is in her possession are all in the man's possession, and in claims, the word of the possessor is accepted. Unlike items specific to women, because the opposing circumstance is stronger. And this dispute, whether during marriage or after separation, makes no difference in the ruling. Explanation of the issue: If husband and wife dispute over household items, each claiming ownership, and there is no evidence: items befitting men are given to the man after he takes an oath, such as turban, hat, books, cloak, bow, weapons. Items befitting women are given to the woman after she takes an oath, such as dupatta, headband, burqa, jewelry. Items befitting both are given to the man, because the man possesses the woman and her possessions, and the possessor's word is accepted. However, items specific to women go to the woman because although the husband has apparent possession, the woman's possession is stronger, like two disputing over a cloth, one wearing it and the other holding its sleeve — the one wearing it has the stronger claim. The author of Hidayah says this applies whether the dispute occurs during marriage or after separation.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the Hanafi school on a common domestic dispute. The passage explains that when spouses disagree over household items and neither has proof, the items are allocated based on customary suitability. The principle is that in such claims, the apparent circumstance (zahir al-hal) is considered a witness. Items traditionally associated with men (e.g., turban, sword) go to the husband, those associated with women (e.g., jewelry, veil) go to the wife, and items suitable for both (e.g., household utensils) go to the husband because he is considered the possessor of his wife and her possessions. The exception is that the wife's possession of her specific items is stronger than the husband's general possession, so she gets them. The ruling applies whether the spouses are still married or have separated.

Key Terms
قابض (qabid): The person who has actual or constructive possession of an item.
ظاہر حال (zahir al-hal): The apparent circumstance or customary indication that points to ownership or entitlement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if spouses dispute over a vessel (bratan) and neither has proof, to whom is it given?
A) The wife
B) The husband
C) It is divided equally
D) It is given to the one who swears an oath

Key Takeaway
In spousal disputes over household items without evidence, Hanafi law allocates items based on customary suitability and possession, giving the wife her specific items due to stronger possession.
